Title: Power production and turbine response during yaw-based wind farm

DTU Wind Energy-M-0211

DTU Wind Energy is a department of the Technical University of Denmark 
with a unique integration of research, education, innovation and public/private 
sector consulting in the field of wind energy. Our activities develop new 
opportunities and technology for the global and Danish exploitation of wind 
energy. Research focuses on key technical-scientific fields, which are central 
for the development, innovation and use of wind energy and provides the 
basis for advanced education at the education. 
 
We have more than 240 staff members of which approximately 60 are PhD 
students. Research is conducted within nine research programmes organized 
into three main topics: Wind energy systems, Wind turbine technology and 
Basics for wind energy.

January 2018 – July 2018

Preface
This thesis is submitted in partial fulﬁllment of the requirements for obtaining a MSc at the
Technical University of Denmark (DTU) and the Carl von Ossietzky University of Olden-
burg (UniOl). The project was carried out at the department of Wind Energy, DTU from
February 2018 until July 2018. Supervisors on the thesis project were Søren Juhl Andersen,
Tuhfe G¨oçmen, Stephan Voß and Martin K¨uhn.
I am very grateful for the the many fruitful discussions and the supervision of Søren Juhl
Andersen, who I could always pass by for many questions. I am also grateful for Tuhfe
G¨oçmen who invited me many times to Risø. In addition to their supervision, I am grateful
for the access gained to the measurement data thanks to Brian Naughton. The data was
collected at the Sandia Scaled Wind Farm Technology (SWiFT) facility as part of the US
Department of Energy Wind Energy Technologies Oﬃce funded Atmosphere to Electrons
(A2e) program. I am also grateful for the discussion with Torben Krogh Mikkelsen about

the polynomial chaos expansion theory. Furthermore, the author is also gratefully for the
implementation of the code for the Mann model ﬁt written by Alfredo de Peˆna.

==PAGE:4==
Abstract
In recent years the use of wind energy has been steadily growing and, with it, the demand
to increase the power output of a wind farm. The wind turbines operate in the wake of the
upwind turbine, causing power losses within a wind farm. Intentionally yawing the turbine
has been the focus of recent studies as one method to reduce the eﬀect of the wake. This
thesis aims to provide a fundamental understanding of applying a yaw-based wind farm
control and aims to highlight the main challenges. In order to perform yaw control three
aspects need to be thoroughly investigated with regard to diﬀerent yaw conﬁgurations: the
wake characteristics, the turbine response and the implementation of a yaw based wind
farm control. This will be accomplished through the use of measurement data, numerical
results and engineering tools. The numerical results are obtained with the ﬂowsolver El-
lipsys3D and LES, from which the wake characteristics have been analyzed. In addition,
the FLORIS model has been used to determine the wake characteristics analytically. The
input parameters used for the numerical simulation and the FLORIS model are based on
the measurement data obtained at the full scale ﬁeld test conducted at the SWiFT facility.
The measurement data showed a deﬂection of the wake center with regard to the yaw angle.
These ﬁndings were comparable with the results of the numerical simulation. In addition,
the numerical simulation showed that the wake has an asymmetric behaviour due to the
shear proﬁle. The wake center has a larger deﬂection at negative yaw angles compared to
positive yaw angles. This has not been captured by the FLORIS model, which also can
not predict the velocity deﬁcit within the near wake region. It was also observed that this
results into a higher power output for the downstream wind turbine. Furthermore, a low
correlation coeﬃcient was found between the thrust and the instantaneous wake at multiple
downstream distances. This indicates that at this case study the wake could not be steered
instantaneously. Through the use of the numerical simulation and the aerolastic tool Flex5
the response of the wind turbine at multiple downstream distances was determined. The
analyzes of the DEL showed that the upstream wind turbine is inﬂuenced by the yaw an-
gle. In addition, it showed that the DEL of the downstream turbine is inﬂuenced by the
deﬂected wake and the yaw angle of the downstream turbine. Depending on the yaw con-
ﬁguration and the spacing, the DEL was increased or decreased. With the obtained data
from the aerolastic tool Flex5 a model has been created which combines the fatigue loads
and the power output. The model is build using the polynomial chaos expansion theory
and showed that a high power gain due to yaw steering was achieved at close spacing, re-
quiring a large negative yaw angle for the upstream turbine and a slight positive yaw angle
for the downstream turbine. At larger spacing the power gain was reduced and with it the
magnitude of the yaw angle for the upstream and downstream turbine. Furthermore, it was
also identiﬁed that a reduction of the DEL was obtained at the cost of power production.

6.10
Load based Optimization with respect to the DEL of the ﬂapwise bending moment and the
DEL of the tower bending moment for the upstream and downstream turbine. The weight-
ing is given as follows: [P Tot, FlapM Turb1, FlapM Turb2, TBBM MY Turb1, TBBM
MY Turb2]. The initial weighting is [1, 0, 0, 0, 0] which optimizes only for the power.
The optimization was performed by reducing the weight for the power and increasing the
weight for a speciﬁc DEL as follows: [1 −dw/10, 0, dw/10, 0, 0] with dwn+1 = dwn + 1
and dw1 = 0.
The optimization which included the DEL of the upstream and down-
stream turbine was done as follows: [1/4 −dw/10/4, dw/40, dw/40, dw/40, dw/40] with
dwn+1 = dwn + 1 and dw1 = 0. The lines are shown with a change in the weight of steps
of 0.2 to reduce the amount of lines. Squared Dots:

1
Introduction
In recent years the use of wind energy was steadily growing and, with it, the demand to
increase the power output of a wind farm. The wind turbines operate in the wake of the
upwind turbine, causing power losses within a wind farm. Intentionally yawing the turbine
was the focus of recent studies as one method to reduce the eﬀect of the wake. The ob-
jective is to intentionally change the trajectory of the wake in order to increase the power
output of the downstream turbines. This can potentially increase the power output of the
total wind farm. Intentionally steering the wake has emerged to be a promising method to
improve the AEP of the wind farm Gebraad et al. (2017) [32].
However, in order to apply yaw-based wind-farm control, a thorough understanding of the
wake and the loading on the wind turbine components will be necessary. The characteristics
of the wake have been analyzed in numerous studies. Initial studies used wind tunnel exper-
iments to analyze the prospect to redirect the wake. Dahlberg & Medici (2003) [55] showed
that a total power gain of 10% was achieved at uniform inﬂow condition. Later, Medici &
Alfredson (2006) [54] quantiﬁed the velocity in the far-wake region at multiple downwind
locations. During their measurements the wake was deﬂected and they observed additional
vortex shedding at large yaw angles.
More recently, studies were conducted estimating
the wake deﬂection downstream at diﬀerent turbulence and shear conditions Bart et al.
(2018) [6]. Numerical simulations were also used to investigate the deﬂection of the wake,
Jimenez et al. (2010) [43]. They have studied the eﬀect of the wake deﬂection with respect
to the thrust coeﬃcient. It was identiﬁed that the wake deﬂection is the highest for turbines
with high thrust coeﬃcients. Fleming et al. (2014) [29] evaluated diﬀerent techniques to
redirect the wake and has shown that the yaw misalignment has the most promising eﬀect.
Furthermore, the eﬀect of diﬀerent atmospheric conditions have been analyzed in Vollmer
et al. (2016) [86]. The strongest improvement of the wind farm performance as a result of
wake redirection occurs in stable conditions.
Succeeding the experimental and numerical studies, engineering models have been build to
facilitate the possibility to implement wake steering in control strategies and wind farm
development. The ﬁrst model is developed by Jimenez et al. (2010) [43] and predicts the
wake deﬂection as function of the yaw angle and the thrust coeﬃcient, assuming a top-hat
proﬁle for the wake velocity deﬁcit. This model was combined with FLORIS((FLOw Redi-
rection and Induction in Steady-State) an engineering wake model separating the wake in
diﬀerent zones that recovers at diﬀerent rates, described in Gebraad et al.(2016) [31]. This
is a well-known model used to predict wake trajectory deviation. It is a parametric model
based on wind turbine position and yaw angle to predict steady-state wake characteristics
(Thomas et al. (2017)[77]). This model was further extended with the wake model devel-
oped in Bastankhah and Porte-Agel(2014, 2016) [8] [9]. The FLORIS model model includes
the turbulence intensity to describe the wake recovery. The FLORIS model was used to
1
==PAGE:25==
optimize the Prinses Amalia Windpark in Fleming et al. (2016) [30] and revealed that an
increase in the power output of 7.7% is possible through yaw control. In addition, Fleming
even suggested to include yaw-control in the design process of new wind farms. Gebraad et
al. (2017) [32] indicated a power increase of 13% and achieved similar results using a high
ﬁdelity CFD simulation and the FLORIS model.
Full-scale ﬁeld tests have also been performed to prove the possibility to steer the wake. A
full-scale ﬁeld test was conducted at the SWiFT facility in the United States, operated by
Sandia in collaboration with NREL [27]. During the full-scale ﬁeld test one turbine was
mounted with a DTU spinner nacelle LiDar and was operating at diﬀerent yaw angles. In
Fleming et al. (2017) [25] the results from the ﬁeld tests indicate good agreement between
the FLORIS model and the observations. In another full-scale ﬁeld test (Fleming et al.
(2017) [26]) performed at the Rudong Wind Farm in China showed encouraging results.
Although limited data was available from the ﬁeld test, an improvement in the power out-
put of two turbines with a spacing of S = 14R and S = 17R was observed.
Despite the numerous studies on the characteristics of the wake, the loading eﬀects on the
wind turbine components during yaw control need to be further analyzed. The eﬀect of
the loading on the operational cost and the maintenance cost is crucial for the success to
apply a yaw based wind farm control in the future. Due to the redirection of the wake the
fatigue load on the downwind turbine can be signiﬁcantly alleviated. Thomson & Sorensen
(1999) [78] illustrated that an increase of turbulence increases the fatigue loads up to 15%
with respect to the ambient inﬂow conditions. The eﬀect of the turbulence can be reduced
when the wake is redirected. Other studies, such as Kragh & Hansen (2014) [47] and Za-
lkind & Pao (2016) [90], demonstrated a decrease in the blade loads for certain yaw angles,
whereas an increase at other components such as the drive-train and the tower were ob-
served. Damian et al. (2017) [18] concludes in contrast to other studies that the fatigue
loads do not necessarily decrease with increasing yaw angles.
Creating a model that predicts the eﬀect of the operational cost and the maintenance cost
with respect to the yaw oﬀset is essential to determine the potential of a yaw based wind
farm control. Current studies have only implemented the power gain in the cost model
(Gebraad et al. (2016) [31]). Van Dijk et al. (2016) [84] has developed a computational
framework, based on the FLORIS model and CCBlade, to analyze the the impact of yaw
control on the power output and the loading. Their results show that a decrease in loading
is possible at the reduction of the power production.
This thesis aims to provide a better understanding of applying a yaw-based wind farm
control and aims to highlight the main challenges. In order to perform yaw control three
aspects need to be thoroughly investigated with regard to diﬀerent yaw conﬁgurations: the
wake characteristics, the turbine response and the implementation of a yaw based wind
2
==PAGE:26==
farm control. This results into the following three main objectives of this thesis:
• Identify the wake characteristics of a yawed turbine through the use of measurement
data, numerical data and engineering tools.
• Acquire a fundamental understanding of the turbine response with respect of DEL
and power output at diﬀerent yaw angles and downstream distances.
• Create a reliable model to predict the equivalent loads and the power production for
the upstream and the downstream turbine.
This will be done with multiple LES simulations, the FLORIS model and measurement
data obtained during a full-scale ﬁeld test conducted at the SWiFT facility in the United
States [27]. During the full-scale ﬁeld test a V27 turbine was mounted with a DTU spin-
ner nacelle LiDar and was operating at diﬀerent yaw angles. The LES simulation will be
performed at multiple yaw angles, from which the loads at several distances will be deter-
mined. This gives a unique opportunity to analyze the wake behaviour through the use of
measurement data, numerical results and an engineering model. The turbine response was
investigated through the numerical simulation. Chapter 2 describes the methodology and
the tools used to investigate the three challenges. The FLORIS model and its capabilities
is presented in section 2.3 and the setup of the LES simulation in Ellipsys3D is illustrated
in section 2.2. Finally, the procedure to develop a model based on the polynomial chaos
expansion theory is described in section 2.4.
The SWiFT facility and the setup of the
full-scale ﬁeld test is described in more detail in Chapter 3, including the input for the
numerical simulation and the FLORIS model.
The analysis of the wake characteristics
and the turbine response was conducted by imitating the inﬂow conditions at the SWiFT
facility while using an aerolastic model of the V27 turbine. The aforementioned tools are
respectively used to analyze the wake characteristics of a single turbine in Section 4.1 and
in Section 4.2 for two turbines. In addition, the eﬀect of the yaw conﬁguration on the
wake meandering is investigated in Section 4.3. This was done to obtain a fundamental
comprehension of the physics involved to steer the wake. The response of the turbine was
investigated with the numerical simulation performed in Ellipsys3D in Chapter 5. This was
done in combination with the aerolastic tool Flex5 developed at the Technical University
of Denmark by Stig Øye [63]. This gives the opportunity to determine the driving factors
inﬂuencing the fatigue loads. The ﬁnal chapter 6 focuses on connecting the behavior of
the wake and the response of the turbine to determine the optimal operational conditions.
This paves the path to develop a cost model to improve the power output of the wind farm,
including the eﬀect on the fatigue loads.
3
==PAGE:27==
2
Methodology
This chapter provides a detailed description of the tools used to evaluate the characteristics
of the wake and the behaviour of the wind turbine. These tools will be used to evaluate the
case study described in Chapter 3. At ﬁrst the theoretical background related to steering
the wake is given to ensure a fundamental understanding of the analysis conducted in
the following chapters. This is followed by Section 2.2, which contains the background and
theory of the numerical simulations performed in Ellipsys3D. It illustrates the approach used
to introduce turbulence into the domain and gives an explanation of the implementation of
the Actuator Line method in Ellypsis3D. The numerical simulation will be used to evaluate
the wake dynamics and the turbine response. The wake behaviour will also be analyzed
with an engineering tool, the FLORIS model. It is a well-known model used to predict
wake trajectory deviation. An overview of the FLORIS model and its capabilities is given
in Section 2.3. The last model is a method to create a response surface capable to predict
the behaviour of the turbine with regard to the power and the loading, obtained with the
numerical simulation. The model is build using the polynomial chaos expansion theory,
which generates a polynomial model. The construction and the theory of the polynomial
chaos expansion theory is included in Section 2.4.
2.1
Theoretical Background
This section describes the theoretical background of the main concepts to steer the wake.
It provides the reader with a fundamental understanding of the mechanisms involved dur-
ing yaw steering. At ﬁrst, the development of the wake and the diﬀerent regions will be
elaborated. This will be followed by a short description of the main aerodynamic eﬀects of
a rotor in yaw and the driving factors to deﬂect the wake. Furthermore, the counter-vortex
pair will be elaborated in the last section. This will ease the process to comprehend the
analyses conducted in Chapter 4 for the wake dynamics and in Chapter 5 for the turbine
response. Furthermore, in this thesis a negative yaw angle indicates that the turbine is
turned counter-clock wise and a positive yaw angle indicates that the turbine is rotated
clockwise.
2.1.1
Near and Far Wake Region
The development of the wake is shown in Figure 2.1, indicating the near wake and the
far wake region within the wake. Within the near wake region the geometry of the rotor
determines the shape of the wake proﬁle. Here, the axial pressure gradient is crucial for
the development of the wake deﬁcit [67]. Root vortices are also present in the near wake
and, typically, have a short lifespan [62]. Furthermore, the velocity deﬁcit is the highest
in the near wake region behind the rotor plane. In the far wake region, the geometry of
the rotor becomes less important and the proﬁle is inﬂuenced by the ambient turbulence.
4
==PAGE:28==
Due to the turbulent mixing in the shear layer between the ambient air and the wake, the
wake expands and the velocity gradually recovers further downstream in the far wake [62].
This leads to a lower velocity deﬁcit within the far wake region. Furthermore, the velocity
deﬁcit and the near wake region is dependant on the thrust and the ambient turbulence
intensity. A lower ambient turbulence intensity leads to less turbulent mixing between the
shear layer and the ambient air, whereas the opposite occurs at a high turbulence intensity.
It is often the case that the turbulence intensity is low at atmospheric stable conditions
and high at atmospheric unstable conditions. Keane et al. [45] demonstrated that the wake
can be modelled with a double Gaussian proﬁle within the near wake and a single Gaussian
proﬁle in the far wake.
Figure 2.1: Wake development of a horizontal-axis wind turbine, adapted from Sanders [67]. The wake follows a
double Gaussian proﬁle in the near wake and a single Gaussian proﬁle in the far wake. Mixing occurs between the
boundary of the wake and the ambient air. 1
2.1.2
Rotor Aerodynamics of a Wind Turbine in Yaw
Yawing the turbine indicates that the incoming ﬂow is not aligned with the rotor axis. In a
yawed conﬁguration, the ﬂow is not symmetric around the rotor axis and the wake develops
around a diﬀerent axis. The angle between the rotor axis and the free-stream velocity is
denoted by ψ. The wake follows the direction indicated as the wake skew axis, which has
a skew angle χ with the rotor axis, illustrated in Figure 2.2. Here θ is the azimuthal angle
with θ0 at the upper part of the rotor increasing positively with a clockwise movement of
the blade. Multiple models exist to describe the skew angle. One model will be further
elaborated in Appendix E during the description of the unsteady BEM model.
1Original ﬁgure was adapted to add the proﬁle of the wake
5
==PAGE:29==
Figure 2.2: Notations used for a rotor yawed clock-

"upwind" region indicate the regions with a high lo-
cal axial induction uz and a low local thrust (Fn).
χ is the angle between the rotor axis and the wake
axis. Figure is based on Branlard et al. (2017) [12].

Figure 2.3: Illustration of the eﬀect on axial induc-

viewed from above. [12].
A uz,2 has a higher in-
duction due to more "positive" contributions of the
vortex rings
Figure 2.2 indicates a "downwind" region and a "upwind" region. In the "downwind" region
the blade is expected to have a higher axial induction and lower normal loads compared to
the other region. Due to the diﬀerence in loading in both regions, the blade is expected to
have a restoring moment. The diﬀerence in the axial induction is visualized in Figure 2.3
by considering the wake vorticity. Assuming a large tip-speed ratio and a constant rotor
rotation, the wake vorticity will be inﬂuenced by the tip-vortices which is concentrated
in a cylindrical wake envelop [12]. The main contribution on the induction perpendicular
to the rotor will be the tangential vorticity. A "positive" contribution indicates that the
vortex ring creates an induction. Figure 2.3 indicates that the "downwind" side receives
mainly positive contributions, whereas the "upwind" side receives positive and negative
contributions. This leads to the induction in the "downwind" region (uz,2) being higher
than the induction in the "upwind" region (uz,1).
2.1.3
Wake Deﬂection
As mentioned in the previous section, steering the wind turbine leads to an asymmetric
loading on the rotor blades. Besides creating an asymmetric loading, the total thrust also
decreases, as demonstrated in Bastankhah and Porte-Agel.
(2016) [9].
They have also
illustrated that the wake deﬂection grew with increasing yaw angle which can be explained
by the conservation of momentum. A yawed turbine exerts a lateral force on the incoming
ﬂow. Due to the conservation of momentum, the lateral force induces a spanwise wake
velocity. This leads to the deﬂection of the wake to one side. This is illustrated in Figure 2.4
and in Figure 2.5 for the case of ψ = -30[◦]

2.1.4
Counter Vortex Pair
Another phenomena of a yawed turbine is the formation of a counter vortex pair (CVP)
which forces the wake to have a kidney-shaped cross section in the far-wake region. The for-
mation of the kidney-shaped wake due to the counter-vortex pair is illustrated in Figure 2.6.
A possible mechanism that forms the CVP is described in Bastankhah and Porte-Agel
(2016) [9] and is illustrated in the schematic shown in Figure 2.7.
Figure 2.6: Wake shape deformation due to the CVP. Black circles
indicate the rotor area on each plane. Gray area indicates the shape
of the wake [41]
Figure
2.7:
Illustration
of
the
mechanism forming the CVP [9]
In order to describe the mechanism that forms the CVP, the incoming ﬂow is assumed to
be uniform and the ground eﬀects are ignored. The continuity equation is given in Equa-
tion 2.1. In the far-wake region the v-component becomes very small and can be neglected
under a non-yawed condition. However, as can be seen in Figure 2.5, the v-component
7
==PAGE:31==
increases under yawed condition and can not be ignored. As described before, the increase
in the lateral velocity component is due to the thrust T · sin(ψ). If the yaw angle is large
enough, the stream-wise velocity, induced by T ·cos(ψ), becomes negligible compared to the
lateral component. The continuity equation in the far wake can be reduced to Equation 2.2.
This indicates that to conserve the continuity at large yaw angles the term δv
δy needs to be
balanced out with δw
δz .

The horizontal line in Figure 2.7 is the symmetry line with v ̸= 0 and w = 0. Point A
indicates where v is at its maximum and point C indicates the maximum velocity deﬁcit.
Furthermore, in region I the term δv
δy is above zero indicating that δw

region II, the term δv
δy is below zero and, thus, the term δw
δz > 0. This means that w becomes
negative in region I and positive in region II. Following this reasoning, the circulation in
the lower half of the turbine can be explained [9]. Due to the CVP, the wake is forced to
have a kidney shape, illustrated in Figure 2.6. This could potentially cause the curled wake
to wrap around the turbine. In addition, it may cause the wake to ’miss’ more of the rotor
area of the downstream turbine.
2.2
Large Eddy Simulation
The LES simulation was performed with Ellipsys3D, a 3D CFD solver with a block-
structured ﬁnite volume approach [37]. It was developed at the Technical University of
Denmark and at the National Laboratory for Sustainable Energy (Risø) in Denmark by
Michelsen (1992) [56] and by Sørensen (1995) [71]. Within Ellipsys3D, the rotor was mod-
elled as an actuator line and the axial forces are deﬁned with BEM. This requires tabulated
airfoil data of the rotor blades to determine the local lift and drag.
2.2.1
Basic Equations
Most engineering tools that model the behaviour of ﬂuids are based on the Navier-Stokes
equations and the continuity equation, illustrated in Equation 2.3 and in Equation 2.4.
Here, u is the velocity, x is the position vector, PPres. is the pressure, ρ is the density, ν
is the kinematic viscosity, fi are external body forces, t is the time and the notations and
i & j are the directional components. Sij is the strain rate tensor shown in Equation 2.5.
There are two methods to solve these equations. Firstly, solving it directly is known as
Direct Numerical Simulation (DNS). And secondly, solving the equation with the use of a
model which is mostly done with the Reynolds Averaged Navier-Stokes (RANS) model or
the Large Eddy Simulation (LES) model that models the small scale turbulence’s. Other
models exist but will not be highlighted. In this case study, LES simulations have been
performed with the numerical solver Ellipsys3D.

2.2.2
Ellipsys3D
Within Ellipsys3D the LES technique was used. This is a turbulence model that calculates
turbulence characteristics by decomposing it into large and small scales. Whereas large
turbulence scales are simulated directly by the Navier-Stokes equations, small turbulences
are modelled. The eﬀect of turbulence eddies smaller than a predeﬁned grid size, δx, are
modelled using a subgrid-scale model. Here, the ﬂuid was simulated to be incompressible
and was simulated with the ﬁltered Navier-Stokes equation. The equation of the incom-
pressible Navier-Stokes equation after applying a low-pass ﬁlter is given in Equation 2.7
and the continuity equation is given in Equation 2.6.

Ellipsys3D solves the above shown equations to calculate the large scale and small scale
eddies in general curvilinear coordinates by using a block-structured ﬁnite-volume ap-
proach [70]. It is formulated in a collocated grid arrangement using the variables for pressure
9
==PAGE:33==
and velocity. A hybrid scheme formed of a third order QUICK schemes [49] and 4th-order
central diﬀerencing schemes are used to discretize the non-linear terms. Furthermore, El-
lipsys3D is coupled with a turbine model to include the body forces in the Navier-Stokes
equation. This can be seen in Equation 2.7, where a number of forces have been added for
the wind turbine, the turbulence and the prescribed boundary layer.
2.2.3
Prescribed Boundary Layer
The atmospheric boundary layer can be modeled in two ways. It can be modelled by using
body forces across the entire computational domain that deﬁnes an arbitrary wind shear
proﬁle. This gives the possibility to model the shear proﬁle and the atmospheric turbulence
independently [83]. The second method is based on the modelled turbulence ﬂuctuations
and the boundary condition at the bottom of the numerical domain. Given enough time
the ﬂow will develop a shear boundary layer.
2.2.4
Actuator Line Modelling
To include the inﬂuence of the wind turbine, Ellispys3D is coupled with the wind turbine
model using the actuator line (AL) model. The AL was developed by Sørensen & Shen
(2002) [72] and implemented in the general aerolastic tool by Mikkelsen et al. (2003) [57]
and Troldborg (2009) [80]. The AL model calculates and imposes the body forces (fWT )
along rotating lines in the numerical domain which will be used in the Navier-Stokes equa-
tion, representing the aerodynamic loads of the rotor blades. The aerodynamic loads are
calculated by coupling it with the aeroalastic tool Flex5 [63]. Flex5 is an aerolastic tool
able to calculate the deﬂections and load response of the entire turbine. The coupling is
performed by transferring the forces calculated with Flex5 to Ellipsys3D [70]. In each time
step the velocities from Ellipsys3D are transferred back to Flex5. With the transferred
velocities from Ellipsys3D the velocity triangle at each blade can be determined. An il-
lustration of the velocity triangle is shown in Figure 2.8. This provides the local angle of
attack from which the lift and drag forces are calculated, with Equation 2.11. Here eL & eD
are the unit vectors parallel to the lift and the drag forces. This requires the aerodynamic
characteristics of the airfoil at each blade segment as tabulated data. The body forces
from the blade segment are smeared as a Gaussian distribution across a few cells to avoid
singularities.
10
==PAGE:34==
Figure 2.8: Velocity Triangle of a local blade segment with
lift (FL) and drag (FD) along the unit vector (eL & eD)
adapted from Troldborg (2009) [80] AOA: Angle of Attack,
VRel: relative velocity,VRot: rotational velocity, uz Local
induced velocity, βp Pitch Angle, θp Twist Angle, φ Flow
Angle
(FL, FD) = 1
2ρV 2
relc(CleL, CDeD)
(2.11)
The advantage of applying the AL method to represent individual blades is that fewer grid
points are required to model the inﬂuence of the blade compared to a fully resolved blade
geometry [70]. Detailed studies of the wake structures, such as the tip and root vortices, are
possible with the AL method with a low amount of grid points. Nevertheless, the drawback
of this method is that it requires tabulated data of the airfoil at each segment [70].
2.2.5
Turbulence Mann Box
The atmospheric turbulence is introduced to the Navier-Stokes equation by imposing body
forces into the ﬂow usually applied in a plane upstream of the turbine [35]. The body
forces are obtained from the Mann-model developed by Mann (1994 [52], 1998 [53]), which
generates turbulent ﬂuctuations. The Mann-model generates a three-dimensional ﬁeld of the
three velocity components u, v and w based on a linearization of the in-compressible Navier-
Stokes equation. The generated turbulence assumes Taylor’s frozen hypothesis. In order
to generate the turbulence ﬁeld the spectral-tensor is matched to the statistics in a neutral
atmosphere. The spectral tensor contains information of the spectra, the cross-spectra and
the coherence’s. Furthermore, the Mann model uses the rapid distortion theory (RDT) in
combination with the "eddy lifetime" to give a description of the amount of shear, giving
the turbulence an an-isotropic character. The generated turbulence of the Mann model
is homogeneous, an-isotropic and stationary [70]. The model requires as an input three
parameters that deﬁnes the turbulence length scale, an estimation of the eddy lifetime and
the energy dissipation. This is done with the turbulence parameters αTurb · ϵ2/3, LTurb and
Γ as an input. The parameter αTurb is the kolmogorov constant, ϵ is the rate of viscous
dissipation of speciﬁc turbulent kinetic energy, LTurb is the turbulence length scale and Γ
is a measure of turbulence an-isotropy.
2.3
FLORIS Model
The engineering model used to analytically predict the wake trajectory deviation is known as
the FLORIS model (FLOw Redirection and Induction in Steady-State). It is a parametric
11
==PAGE:35==
model based on the wind turbine position and the yaw angle to predict the steady-state wake
characteristics [77] developed at NREL and TU Delft. The python code of the FLORIS
model was obtained from Github and the version ’0.1.1’ was used 3. The model is used
to investigate the wake characteristics in comparison to the results from the measurement
campaign at the SWiFT facility and the numerical simulation explained in section 2.2.
2.3.1
Wake Models
Currently, the FLORIS model is implemented with the Jensen model (Jensen (1983) [42]),
the multizone wake model (Gebraad et al.
2016 [31]) and the self-similar wake model
with contributions from (Dilip & Porte-Agel(2017) [19]); Niayifar & Porte-Agel(2016) [61];
Abkar & Porte-Agel (2015) [2] and Bastankhah & Porte-Agel(2014 [8], 2016 [9])). During
a ﬁeld test conducted by the National Renewable Energy Laboratory (NREL) in Golden,
the possibility to steer the wake was demonstrated and the various models within the
FLORIS model were validated in Annoni et al. (2018) [4]. The campaign has shown that
the Gaussian wake model provided with the best representation of the wake at diﬀerent
atmospheric conditions. An overview of the diﬀerent models is shown below, with a detailed
description of the Gaussian wake model which will be used for further analysis.
Jensen Model
The Jensen model is developed by Jensen (1983) and is based on the assumption that there
is a steady inﬂow and a linear wake expansion. Furthermore, the model assumes a uniform
velocity distribution downstream of the turbine and models the turbine as an actuator disk
with a uniform axial loading in a steady uniform ﬂow.
Multizone Model
This model is a modiﬁcation of the Jensen model by Gebraad van Wingerden (2014) and
classiﬁes the wake in three zones: near wake zone, far wake zone and mixing zone. In
addition, it includes the eﬀect of partial wake overlap present in yawed condition. This
is done by combining the eﬀect of each wake zone of the upstream turbine.
However,
the multizone wake model is not sensitive to the turbulence intensity or added turbulence
generated from a wind turbine.
Gaussian Wake Model
This model characterizes the wake with a 2D Gaussian wake in the y and z direction
to determine the velocity deﬁcit. Furthermore, it is sensitive to added turbulence based
on turbine operation and the atmospheric stability.
The velocity deﬁcit is given with
Equation 2.12. In the latter equation δ is the deﬂection of the wake in the y-direction
3https://github.com/WISDEM/FLORIS
12
==PAGE:36==
and zh is the hub height. It is assumed that the wake remains at hub height as the vertical
displacement of the wake center is small for small yaw angles [9]. Due to its simplicity, a 2D
Gaussian distribution was selected to describe the wake since the presence of Counter Vortex
Pair is not strong for angles smaller than 30[◦] [9]. Diﬀerent wake width are considered in
the y and z direction, given by σy and σz. The wake has diﬀerent width in the vertical
and horizontal direction due to the elliptical shape of the frontal area of a yawed wind
turbine [9]. The Gaussian wake model divides the wake into two regions: the near-wake
region and the far-wake region shown in Figure 2.9. In the near-wake region the potential
core of the wake is not eﬀected by the free-stream velocity, whereas in the far-wake region
the core of the wake is eﬀected by it.
The parameter C is the velocity deﬁcit at the
wake centre, normalized with the incoming velocity shown in Equation 2.13. Furthermore,
the width of the wake grows further downstream.
This is given by Equation 2.14 and
Equation 2.15, where the growth rate for σy and σz diﬀer as demonstrated in Abkar &
Porte-Agel (2015) [2]. The parameter kz and ky is the growth rate of the wake in the
vertical or horizontal direction. The turbulence intensity is linked with the wake expansion
through an empirical relationship given in Bastankhah et al. (2014) [8] and Niayifar et al.
(2016) [61], illustrated in Equation 2.16. Currently, the growth rate is similar for kz and
ky in the FLORIS model.

2.4
Surrogate Model
This section describes a method to build a surrogate model which will be used to model the
response of the upwind and downwind turbine with regard to the power and the turbine
loading. The surrogate will be build using the polynomial chaos expansion theory to create
a response surface to capture the dependency of the output as function of the input [59]. In
Murcia et al. (2018) [59] polynomial surrogates have been used to characterize the equiv-
alent loads and the power production of the turbine. Furthermore, the polynomial chaos
expansion theory has also been used in a study conducted by Dimitrov [20] to demonstrate
quick assessments of site-speciﬁc lifetime fatigue loads. In this study, the polynomial chaos
expansion theory will be used to create a response surface of the equivalent loads and
the power output obtained from the numerical simulations described in Section 3.2. The
aim is to create a model which is able to predict the response of the turbine at certain
yaw-conﬁgurations which have not been simulated. In addition, the tool will be used in
Chapter 6 to determine the optimal operation conditions of the case study described in
Chapter 3 to increase the total power output and to build a simpliﬁed cost-model which
incorporates the fatigue loads.
2.4.1
Polynomial Chaos Expansion
The main purpose of a surrogate model is to build a model that deﬁnes the relationship
between the input and the output of a given data set in a fast and accurate manner. There
are multiple ways to create a surrogate model, such as interpolation techniques, response
surface techniques [15], Gaussian Process or Kriging technique [1] and machine learning
techniques [16]. The polynomial chaos expansion (PCE) is an approach to create a poly-
nomial response surface to capture the relation between the output and the input. This
method was developed by Wiener (1938) [87] and was brought back by Ghanem et al.
(1991)
[34]. It is widely used due to its simplicity and fast convergence in comparison
to a full Monte-Carlo simulation [59] based on the original model according to Sudret et
al. (2008) [74]. In addition, PCE training algorithms can be applied to create a sparse
15
==PAGE:39==
surrogate. This will result in the model having, a reduced number of terms that are depen-
dant on multiple variables and thus lead to an eﬃcient model. Furthermore, in the case
of a smooth model, a sparse PCE model is the most eﬃcient with respect to the number
of model evaluations and amount of input dimensions [59]. A brief overview of the main
equations is shown in the next section.
The response of a PCE model can be described as Equation 2.21 dependant on a arbitrary
amount of input variables. This can be re-written as Equation 2.22 according to Soize
et al. [69] based on the theory of Ghanem et al. (1991) [34] to expand any second-order
random variable. As seen in Equation 2.22 the response surface of a model can be described
as the summation of a Hilbertian basis (Ψj), dependant on random values of the vector X
and a corresponding coeﬃcient Sj. A Hilbertian basis is a family of orthogonal polynomials
indicating that the inner dot product of each polynomial sequence equals to zero, such as
the Hermite polynomial. A polynomial family that is dependant on one variable is also
known as a uni-variate polynomial.
SModel = f(X1, X2, ..., Xp)
(2.21)

(2.22)
The general PCE model depends on multiple input variables and is build by a set of mul-
tivariate polynomials. This is obtained by tensor products of the uni-variate polynomials
of each individual input parameter. This indicates that the multivariate polynomial is also
orthogonal. Based on the input of the model a selection has to be made for the polynomial
family, which is shown in Section 2.4.2

The number of unknown coeﬃcients (G) depends on the order of the polynomial used to
describe the model and the amount of input variables. The relation is shown in Equa-
tion 2.23, where p is the order of dimension of the polynomial and q is the number of input
variables of the model [74].
2.4.2
Polynomial Families
As described in Section 2.4.1, a Hilbertian basis is used to model the response surface which
needs to be a sequence of orthogonal polynomials [74]. There are multiple polynomial fam-
ilies which can be used to build a surrogate model, but due to the investigated case study
two polynomial families will be highlighted here, namely the Hermite and the Legendre
polynomial. The ﬁrst polynomial family is the Hermite polynomial which are orthogonal
16
==PAGE:40==
with respect to the Gaussian probability measure and are used when the input parameter
is following a normal distribution. The second polynomial family is the Legendre polyno-
mials which are orthogonal with respect to the uniform probability measure over [-1, 1]
and are used when the input parameter is following a uniform distribution. The Hermite
polynomial (HE) is a family of orthogonal polynomials which is a solution to the diﬀerential
Equation 2.24. It has the following recurrence relationship shown in Equation 2.25, also
known as the three term recurrence relationship. The ﬁrst three Hermite polynomials are
shown in Equation 2.26.

2.5
Summary
The theoretical background and the tools used to evaluate the response of the upstream and
downstream turbine have been showcased in this chapter. In the ﬁrst section, the theory of
the near and the far wake have been described, giving an overview of how the wake develops
downstream. Furthermore, the aerodynamics of a rotor in yaw was illustrated. There it
highlighted that the rotor experiences an azimuthal variation in the blade loading. This is
due to the variation in the induction velocity. In addition, the wake deﬂection is due to a
spanwise velocity component induced by a lateral force of the thrust. Deﬂecting the wake
also leads to kidney-shaped cross section which is caused by the counter vortex pair. The
numerical simulations are performed in Ellipsys3D, a 3D CFD solver with a block-structured
ﬁnite volume approach. Ellipsys3D models, the rotor as an actuator line and the axial forces
are deﬁned with tabulated airfoil data. The FLORIS model is a parametric model based on
the wind turbine position and the yaw angle to predict steady-state wake characteristics.
Finally, the theory to build the surrogate model is explained in detail. The surrogate model
is build with the polynomial expansion theory which will be used to determine the optimal
layout conﬁguration for two turbines in a row. These tools will be used to evaluate the
case study described in Chapter 3 in order to analyze the characteristics of the wake in
Chapter 4 and the response of the wind turbine in Chapter 5 at certain conﬁgurations.
19
==PAGE:43==
3
Case Study
This chapter describes the case study performed to analyze the wake characteristics and
the turbine response. Furthermore, it gives an overview of the generated data to create a
model based on the PCE in order to implement yaw based wind farm control. This will
be accomplished with the measurement data, numerical results and engineering tools. The
numerical results are obtained with Ellipsys3D and the FLORIS model was used to deter-
mine the wake characteristics analytically. The input parameters used for the numerical
simulation and the FLORIS model are based on the measurement data obtained at the
full scale ﬁeld test conducted at the SWiFT facility. Section 3.1 gives a description of the
instrumentation and the measurement campaign. This is followed by Section 3.2 giving a
detailed overview of the simulation cases and the required input parameters. Furthermore,
the input parameters for the FLORIS model are also based on the full-scale ﬁeld test which
are shown in Section 3.3
3.1
SWiFT Facility
In this section the layout of the Scaled Wind Farm Technology (SWiFT) facility will be dis-
cussed. It will include an explanation about the instrumentation used during the full-scale
ﬁeld test to steer the wake. The SWiFT facility is located at the Texas Tech University’s
National Wind Institute Research Center in Lubbock, Texas.
In addition, this chapter
includes the analyses of the measurement data obtained on the 15th of December 2016
between 20:09:37 to 22:52:37 [CST]. The obtained data will be used as an input for the
FLORIS model and the numerical simulation in Section 3.2 and in Section 3.3 respectively.
3.1.1
SWiFT Site Layout
The SWiFT facility is equipped with two 60-m meteorological towers with various sensors
and three highly modiﬁed Vestas V27 wind turbines, which were used during the ﬁeld test.
In addition, the upstream turbine (WTGa1 Turbine in Figure 3.1) is mounted with a nacelle
SpinnerLidar of the Technical University of Denmark (DTU), directed to the rear to charac-
terize the wake. The meteorological tower contains sonic anemometers, cup anemometers,
a wind vane, relative humidity sensors, temperature sensors and barometric sensors. The
two turbines used in this campaign are aligned in the dominant wind direction. The exact
locations of the wind turbines and the meteorological towers are given in Table 1. Mea-
surement data are obtained from WTGa1, METa1 and the mounted nacelle SpinnerLidar.
The analyses have been conducted with measurement data obtained on the 15th of Decem-
ber 2016 between 20:09:37 to 22:52:37 [CST]. A description of the sensors mounted on the
meteorological tower, the turbine and the spinner Lidar is given in the next paragraph.

For the analysis of the wake characteristics, the SpinnerLidar from the Technical University
of Denmark was used. Each scan had a duration of 2 s with 984 data points. The coordinate
system of the Lidar (sx, sy, sz) are measured using a calibrated 3-axis accelerometer. The
origin of the coordinate system of the Lidar was centered around the rotation axis of the
nacelle and the base of the turbine foundation. In addition, the x-y plane of the SWiFT
facility matches the sx-sy plane of the Lidar. Also, the vertical coordinate frame of the
SWiFT facility and the Lidar are matched.
3.1.2
Inﬂow Conditions
As mentioned before, the measurement data is obtained on the 15th of December 20:09:37
to 10:52:37 [CST], indicating that the measurement was conducted at night. The statistical
parameters of the inﬂow condition is given in Table 3.

Table 3: Statistical parameters of the inﬂow condition at the SWiFT Facility on the 15th of December 20:09:37 to
10:52:37 [CST]. σuv,32m and σwdir,32m have been determined with the wind vane or from the angle between the
u-component and the v-component obtained from the sonic anemometer
The atmospheric stability was calculated following the similarity model described in Van
Ulden et al. (1985) [85] using the measurement data from the cup anemometer and the
sonic anemometer. Since the measurements were conducted at night, the atmosphere is
expected to have a stable atmospheric boundary layer. This is also described in Kelly et
al. (2016) [46] where it states that the atmosphere has a high probability to have a stable
condition between [20:00 - 07:00], an unstable condition between [09:00 - 17:00] and a neutral
condition between [08:00 - 09:00] & [18:00 - 19:00]. This means that the surface roughness
could not be derived with the available data and that an approximation had to be made for
the surface roughness in order to determine the Obukhov Length Scale (Monin& Obukhuv
(1954) [58]) with the similarity model. Since the surrounding area is ﬂat with small bushes,
a surface roughness of z0 = 0.01[m] was used. This assumption corresponds with other
studies conducted at the SWiFT facility shown in Churchﬁeld et al. 2016 [14] and Yang et
al. 2015 [89]. During the calculations for the Obukhov Length the speciﬁc heat constant
at constant pressure with cp = 1004 [J 1
kg · 1
K ], according to Berg et al. 2013 [10] and the
air density with ρ = 1.12[ kg
m3 ] was used. According to the classiﬁcation proposed, in P˜ena
et al. (2010) [65] the Obukhov Length from the measurement data ranges between near
neutral-stable condition and stable condition. This means there is a downward buoyant
momentum ﬂux. In addition, it means that the turbulence is suppressed. Moreover, this
indicates that there is a strong wind shear. Finally, during the ﬁeld test the wind turbine
was yawed between [-15◦, 5◦] every 2-3 minutes, illustrated in Figure 3.3. To ensure that the
analysis is conducted with cases where the wind was fully developed at a certain yaw-angle,
the data measured 1 min after a change in the yaw misalignment had to be ﬁltered out.

3.2
Large Eddy Simulation
This section describes the generation of the turbulence box used in the numerical simula-
tion, based on the measurement data showed in Section 3.1. The inﬂow conditions were
determined using the u, v, w - component from the sonic anemometer at hub height at
the SWiFT facility. Furthermore, there will be an overview of the simulation cases used to
analyze the wake characteristics and the turbine response and the development of the PCE
model.
3.2.1
Atmospheric Turbulence Condition
As mentioned in Section 2.2.5, the Mann box requires as an input the turbulence parameters
αTurb·ϵ2/3, LTurb and Γ as an input. These parameters are determined using the time-series
for the u,v,w-component and the cross-spectrum of uw obtained from the sonic anemometer
at a height of 32 m. This resulted into the following values for the parameters: αTurb·ϵ2/3 =
0.03379 m4/3
s2 , LTurb = 156.69 [m] and τ = 3.0516 [-].
The ﬁt of the timeseries of the
measurement to obtain turbulence parameters with the spectra and cross-spectra is shown
in Figure 3.4 as the solid lines. The dotted lines indicate the spectra and the cross-spectra
from the numerical simulation. This indicates that the generated Mann model has the same
turbulence characteristics compared to the measurements from the full-scale ﬁeld test.
24
==PAGE:48==
Figure 3.4: Mann model ﬁt from the u, v, w and uw time series at the SWiFT facility at 32 m. αT urb · ϵ2/3 = 0.03379
[ m4/3
s2
], LT urb = 156.69 m, τ = 3.0516 [-] Solid Lines:
Fitted spectra and cross-spectra from the measurement
data Dots:
Spectra and cross-spectra from the numerical simulation
It should be noted that the turbulence parameters are not the same at diﬀerent heights,
which inﬂuences the wake development and, with that, the velocity deﬁcit within the wake.
Table 4 gives the turbulence parameters at diﬀerent height. It can be seen that the turbu-
lence length scale increases with higher heights. This is due to the increasing wind speed
at larger heights. Furthermore, it can be seen that the term αTurb · ϵ2/3 and Γ reduces at
an increasing height. This indicates that the energy dissipation reduces at a larger height.
3.2.2
Prescribed Boundary Layer
As discussed in Section 2.2.2 body forces are added in the Navier-Stokes equation to create
the boundary layer. With the use of these body forces the boundary layer is forced to follow
a certain proﬁle, which was determined using the measurement data at the SWiFT facility.
The veer proﬁle was set to 0◦, as the deviation in the angle is smaller than ± 5 ◦over
the height. The velocity proﬁle of the LES simulation and the SWiFT facility is shown in
Figure 3.5. Here, it can be seen that the velocity proﬁle from the LES simulation follows
the velocity proﬁle from the measurements as expected.

3.2.3
Simulation Cases
Table 5 shows the simulated cases and the conﬁguration for each case. Here, the same
conﬁguration as at the SWiFT facility was used, which had a spacing of S = 10 R between
the upwind and the downwind turbine.
At each simulation the ﬂowﬁeld was extracted
and used as an input to the aerolastic code Flex5, illustrated in Figure 3.6. The turbines
where the extracted ﬂowﬁeld was used will be referred as "Ghost-Turbines" since they
physically do not eﬀect the ﬂowﬁeld. This enables the possibility to analyze the turbine at
each downstream distance at various yaw angles for the ﬁrst turbine. At each downwind
distance the V27 model was simulated for ψ =[-30◦:2.5◦:30◦] with a Cone Angle of 0◦and
-5◦. A total of 7 cases where simulated in Ellipsys3D with one turbine, four cases were
simulated with 4 turbines and one case was simulated with no turbines. The simulation
cases with a single turbine were used to determine the wake dynamics of each downstream
turbine. The simulation cases with four turbines were used to analyze the eﬀects within a
wind farm. The simulation case with no turbine was used to analyze the response of the
wind turbine at S = 0R.

3.3
FLORIS Model
This section describes the input parameters for the FLORIS model, tabulated in Table 6.
These have been determined with the measurement data of the full scale ﬁeld test and from
the turbine model. The FLORIS model required as an input the parameter P P and P T ,
which are related to the turbine model. They are adjustable parameters that match the
power loss due to yaw misalignment and tilt angle, as explained in Equation 3.1.
cp = 4aT (1 −aT )2ηcos(ψ)P P cos(θTilt)P T
(3.1)
The relation between the power and the yaw angle was determined using the aerolastic tool
Flex5 of the V27 turbine and is illustrated in Figure 3.9. This was done with a uniform
steady inﬂow condition. It was shown for the NREL 5 MW that the value P P & P T are
both equal to 1.88 in Jonkman et al. (2009) [44]. With the known relation between the

3.4
Turbine Model
This section describes the V27 turbine at the SWiFT facility.
A model of the V27 is
necessary for the numerical simulations in Ellipsys3D and in the FLORIS model. It also
includes a description of the method to calculate the equivalent loads.
3.4.1
V27 Model
As mentioned before the aerolastic tool Flex5 is used to determine the loading on the
components of the wind turbine. Flex5 is based on the blade element method and requires
tabulated data of the airfoil data. It uses a modal approach to determine the response and
the loads. A wind turbine model of the V27 was used to simulate all the forces. The V27
model is loosely based on the report by Resor & LeBlanc (2014) [66]. The eigenfrequencies
have been calibrated according to the reported experimental values. Table 7 shows the
eigenfrequencies of the V27 model and the experimental values which were reported. The
main parameters of the V27 wind turbine are given in Table 8, with a rated wind speed
equal to V = 11.74 [m/s] and a rated power of 225 kW. The power coeﬃcient and the thrust
coeﬃcient are given in Figure 3.10 and in Figure 3.11.

3.4.2
Equivalent Loads
According to "Recommended Practices for Wind Turbine Test and Evaluation: 3. Fatigue
Loads", IEA [51] the equivalent load is determined using the so-called rainﬂow counting
method. This reduces the loading to a histogram of full and half cycles with diﬀerent load
ranges [3]. From the histogram a single load value was determined equivalent to a load with
a constant amplitude. This was calculated with Equation 3.2. Here, Seq is the equivalent
load, TTime is the time period, nCycles is the number of cycles, Q0 is the amplitude of the
loading cycle and m is a W¨ohler constant. Furthermore, NCycles is the total amount of
cycles with the response signal. The Matlab code from Adam Nieslony 6 was used which is
in agreement with the "Standard Practices for cycle counting in fatigue analysis" [73]. The
equivalent loads have been determined with the timeseries obtained from Flex5. There the
ﬁrst 100 s have been removed for the analysis.

3.5
Implementation of Chaospy
The PCE-model was build using the python software toolbox Chaospy 7, which is a numer-
ical tool able to perform uncertainty quantiﬁcation using the polynomial chaos expansion
theory and the advanced Monte Carlo method.
It was ﬁrst introduced in Feinberg et
al. (2015) [23]. It gives the opportunity to easily construct tailored algorithms for un-
certainty quantiﬁcation. Furthermore, it gives the user full control over random variable
generation, sampling schemes, response evaluation, numerical integration rules, polynomial
construction and point collocation [23]. The Point Collocation method was used to build
the response surface of the model. It is a non-intrusive method which builds upon the idea
by ﬁtting the polynomial to the input variable by solving a linear system arising from a
statistical regression. The response of a model is build by using orthogonal polynomial
families using the three term recursion relationship as described in the previous section. A
multivariate polynomial expansion in Chaospy was also created by using the tensor product
rule of uni-variate polynomials [23]. The standard statistical linear regression approach is
used to ﬁt the polynomial to the given set of data points. In Section 6 the results of the
PCE-model is analyzed. This is done with a model of the order of p = [3, 4, 5, 6] with the
input variables: the upstream yaw angle (ψ1), the downstream yaw angle (ψ2) and the spac-
ing between the turbines (S). The model that will be created will predict the response of
the upstream and the downstream turbine. Table 9 gives an overview of the various models
created using the PCE method and the corresponding distribution of the input parameter
from which the multivariate polynomial is build. The model build for the upstream turbine
is only dependant on ψ1. The power of the upstream turbine is modeled using Equation 3.1,
shown in Section 3.3. The variable space of the model for the downstream turbine depends
on the yaw angle of the upstream turbine (ψ1), the yaw angle of the downstream turbine
(ψ2) and the downstream distance (S). The response surface of the downstream turbine for
the power output and the equivalent loads was build with a polynomial order of p = [3, 4,
5, 6], which resulted in [20, 35, 54, 85] coeﬃcients respectively. Furthermore, to increase
the stability of the model a running mean was used over the time series obtained from
the Flex5 simulations to increase the amount of data points for the power output with
the corresponding inﬂow angles. This was done by using a window of 10 min shifted every
30 s. The same procedure was conducted to determine the equivalent loads of the wind
turbine components. In addition, the input variables were normalized with ψ1 = [−1, 1],
ψ2 = [−1, 1] and d = [0, 1]. This is to give each parameter the same weight, in order to
avoid that the model is biased towards one parameter. The output variable was also nor-
malized to [0, 1], shown in the code snippet 1 in Appendix I. In addition, a k-fold value of
10 was used to increase the model stability, shown in the code snippet 2 in Appendix I.
Furthermore, the model was created with the numerical data between 3R <= S <= 18R in
order to increase the accuracy for the region of interest. The behaviour of the PCE model
https : //se.mathworks.com/matlabcentral/fileexchange/3026 −rainflow −counting −algorithm
7Chaospy http://chaospy.readthedocs.io/en/master/installation.html#from-source

model for the downstream turbine depends on the yaw angle of the ﬁrst turbine (ψ1), the
yaw angle of the second turbine (ψ2) and the downstream distance (S). For the yaw angle
a normal distribution is used, since this was also observed with the measurement data and
the LES results. For the normal distribution µwdir = 0[◦] and σwdir = 4.95[◦] based on the
distribution of the wind direction in the numerical simulation at hub height is determined.
For the distance a uniform distribution is used, this is since the downstream distance is
ﬁxed at a certain location. For the uniform distribution the lower boundary was set to be 0
and the upper boundary at 1 since the position of the downstream distance is ﬁxed. These
input parameters for the distributions are used as starting values to ease the process to
ﬁnd the coeﬃcients. The coeﬃcients of the PCE model will be determined based on the
regression method.

==PAGE:58==
3.6
Summary
This chapter provides a description of the SWiFT facility, including the layout of the
facility and a description of the measurement data of the full-scale ﬁeld campaign. The
measurement data is used to generate the turbulence box with the Mann model and to
prescribe the boundary layer for the numerical simulation in Ellipsys3D. Furthermore, the
measurement data was used to determine the input parameters for the FLORIS model.
Finally, the steps conducted to develop the surrogate model with the polynomial chaos
expansion theory for this case study is explained. Chapter 4 shows the analysis of the
obtained results with regard to the behaviour of the wake. This will be followed by the
investigation of the turbine response in Chapter 5. Chapter 6 examines the performance of
the developed PCE model with the generated data points in the numerical simulation. It
includes the optimal conﬁguration for two wind turbines with regard to the power and the
loads.
35
==PAGE:59==
4
Wake Dynamics
This chapter analyzes the response of the wake with regard to diﬀerent yaw-conﬁgurations.
The wake characteristics will be analyzed with the use of measurement data, numerical
results and analytic results. As mentioned in Section 2.1, yawing the turbine will lead to a
deﬂection in the wake. In Section 3.1 a full-scale ﬁeld test conducted at the SWiFT facility
is described. With the obtained measurement data multiple numerical simulation’s in El-
lipsys3D were performed at diﬀerent yaw-conﬁgurations. These simulations were performed
by using the turbulence parameters obtained from the ﬁeld-test and the same spacing. The
setup of the numerical simulations is described in Section 2.2 and the same conﬁgurations
were used with the engineering model FLORIS, highlighted in Section 2.3. The analyses
will focus on the complexity of the wake behind the upstream turbine and behind the
downstream turbine. This will be done by investigating the velocity deﬁcit, the turbulence
intensity and the wake deﬂection. In addition, the behaviour of the wake meandering is
evaluated at diﬀerent yaw conﬁgurations.
4.1
Single Turbine
This section contains the analysis of the complexity of the wake due to a single turbine in
yaw. The investigation will be conducted by inspecting the velocity deﬁcit, the turbulence
intensity and the deﬂection. This is done through the use of measurement data described
in Section 3.1, numerical results described in Section 3.2 and the FLORIS model described
in Section 3.3.
4.1.1
Full Scale Field Test
As described in Section 2.1.3, a yawed turbine exerts a lateral force on the incoming ﬂow.
Due to the conservation of momentum, the lateral force induces a spanwise wake velocity.
This leads to the deﬂection of the wake to one side. In order to analyze the behaviour of the
wake at a certain yaw misalignment, care had to be taken with the measurement data as
the turbine was continuously changing the yaw angle shown in Figure 3.3. The yaw heading
was changing every 2-3 minutes; this meant that the data had to be binned into segments
which had a constant yaw heading. In addition, the ﬁrst 20 s of each segment was removed
in order to exclude the time required for the wake to develop after a sudden change in the
yaw angle. Finally, only segments with a time domain larger than 1 min were included. In
addition, the average diﬀerence between the constant yaw heading and the wind direction
was used as the yaw misalignment for the data points (wake center & power) within each
segment during the analysis. Based on Figure 4.1 the measurement data were then binned
in ± 2.5 [◦] for the yaw angle and the wind speed ± 0.5 [ m
s ].
36
==PAGE:60==
Figure 4.1: Scatter histogram of the yaw angle and the wind speed at the SWiFT facility.
The yaw angle was
determined by determining the diﬀerence between the yaw heading of the wind turbine and the wind direction. The
wind speed is the horizontal wind speed measured with the sonic anemometer at 32 m
Figure 4.2 shows the binned power output of the V27 turbine at the SWiFT facility, relative
to the wind speed at diﬀerent yaw-misalignments. It is expected that the power production
reduces at higher yaw angles according to Bastankhah & Porte-Agel [9]. However, no large
diﬀerence was noted of the power performance with regard to the yaw angle in Figure 4.2.
Furthermore, it can be seen that the power curve obtained from the measurement and the
FLORIS model do not match. This can be due to the low amount of data at each condition
and the extremely short time duration of the segments, which can lead to a mismatch in the
power and the yaw heading during the binning process. The parameter (n) is the number
of Lidar scans. This was used since the wake center was determined at each Lidar scan
providing a direct comparison. The total amount of data points for the power is higher.

Figure 4.3 illustrates the power relative to the yaw angles at diﬀerent wind speeds. Here,
the same pattern can be observed in Figure 4.2. The binned measurement data results in a
higher power output compared to the FLORIS model at low wind speeds and under-predicts
at higher wind speeds. The data points with a wind speed range of U∞= 8 ± 0.5 [ m
s ], have
38
==PAGE:62==
the highest amount of data points and the relationship between the power and the yaw
angle resembles the relationship between the power and the yaw angle from the FLORIS
model. The power production reduces at higher yaw angles, which is what is expected [9].
This indicates that for further analysis the data with a wind speed range of U∞= 8 ± 0.5
[ m
s ] will be used to determine the wake deﬂection and the wake boundary.
Since the measurement data consists of a 3 hour time period, the dependency to the turbu-
lence intensity could not be investigated. A higher turbulence intensity in the atmospheric
boundary layer will lead to higher turbulent entrainment ﬂuxes in the wake. Consequently,
this results in a faster wake recovery compared to a lower turbulence intensity according to
Wu & Porte-Agel (2012) [88]. The dependency of the power curve related to the turbulence
intensity is described in Peinke et al. (2007) [64] and illustrated in Figure 4.4. During the
measurement campaign a high turbulence intensity was observed TI = 14.3[%]. This could
be another reason why the power is higher than the steady curve at low wind speeds and
lower at higher wind speeds.
Figure 4.4: Typical eﬀects of turbulence on power curves [64]
4.1.2
Velocity Deﬁcit
A diﬀerence was noticeable between the numerical simulation, the FLORIS model and the
measurement data. At ﬁrst, the diﬀerence of the velocity deﬁcit between the numerical
simulation and the FLORIS model are discussed. The diﬀerence between the numerical
simulation and the measurement data are discussed later.
The velocity deﬁcit is illustrated more clearly in Figure 4.5, indicating the relative velocity
U
U∞at hub height. This is crucial as the aim of wake steering is to increase the power
production, which inherently indicates that the relative velocity needs to be increased. The
relative velocity at ψ1 = 0[◦] obtained from the numerical simulation and the FLORIS
model will be analyzed ﬁrst, after which the behaviour of the velocity deﬁcit at diﬀerent
yaw angles will be investigated. The relative velocity will be divided into four regions.
In the ﬁrst region, between S = [-4R, -1R], the relative velocity experiences a reduction

due to the induction zone of the wind turbine. Since the FLORIS model is a steady state
model, the induction zone is not modeled. In the second region, S = [-1R, 0R], the velocity
increases due to the exclusion of the nacelle in the simulations shown in Troldborg et al.
(2017) [81]. This eﬀect is also not visible in the FLORIS model.
In the third region, S = [0R, 5R] the velocity reduces, reaching its minimum around S ≈5R.
This is due to the extraction of the energy from the ﬂowﬁeld. Since the center line velocity
is depicted, the reduction of the relative velocity within the near wake region is presented.
The velocity distribution obtained from the numerical simulation and the FLORIS model
at a downstream distance is illustrated in Figure 4.6. Here, it is clearly visualized that the
FLORIS model is unable to predict the velocity distribution within the near wake region.
As mentioned in Section 2.3, the wake model was derived by Bastankhah & Porte Agel.
(2016) [9]. It states that the proposed wake model does not accurately predict the velocity
deﬁcit within the near wake region due to the assumption of the potential core. The concept
of the potential core assumes that the core slowly diminishes due to the interaction with
the surrounding air and that the central part of the potential core does not interact with
the surrounding air during that process. Nevertheless, this concept is not able to predict
the complexity of the three-dimensional structure within the near wake region Bastankhah
& Porte Agel (2016) [9] state that it successfully determines the characteristics of the be-
ginning of the far wake region. In the last region, region IV with S = [5R, 36R], the relative
velocity slowly increases. This is also known for the far wake region, where the surrounding
air interacts with the wake. There the numerical simulation and the FLORIS model have
a similar behaviour.

Figure 4.5 also illustrates the relative velocity at diﬀerent yaw angles. There it can be seen
that the velocity at the center line reduces at larger yaw angles, which is desired for yaw
steering purposes. Furthermore, the ﬁgure depicts that the velocity deﬁcit is larger at neg-
ative yaw angles compared to positive yaw angles for the numerical results. This related to
the asymmetric behaviour of the wake, which will be discussed in Section 4.1.3. The asym-
metry eﬀect is not captured by the FLORIS model. A similar trend for negative yaw angles
is obtained but for positive yaw angles the relative velocity does not correspond with the
numerical simulations. This highlights the conﬁdence region of the FLORIS model, which
is only able to predict the velocity distribution within the far wake region for negative yaw
angles.
A diﬀerence between the numerical simulation and the measurement is visible in Figure 4.5.
This is related to the diﬀerence of the turbulence parameters and the atmospheric stability
between the numerical simulation and the measurements obtained at the SWiFT facility.
This can be explained when looking at the turbulence with respect to the height. Due to
the relative short time duration of the numerical simulation, large turbulence structures
were not simulated. In order to provide an accurate comparison, the time-series of the
measurement data was de-trended with a sampling rate equal to the time duration of the
largest length scale fs = k ∗u∞observed in the numerical simulation, shown in Figure C.1.
Figure 4.7 highlights the comparison of the de-trended time series of the measurements
and the numerical simulation. A large diﬀerence in the standard deviation at lower heights
and a smaller discrepancy at higher heights, 7.3% at 32 m can be distinguished. This can
be due to the generation of the turbulence ﬁeld with the Mann model, which was done
using the turbulence parameters at 32 m height as described in Section 3.2.1 for the entire
41
==PAGE:65==
Figure 4.7: Comparison of the standard deviation of the numerical simulation and the SWIFT facility based on
the de-trended time-series. Blue & Red Area: Shaded area indicates the maximum and the minimum σ after
calculating the running standard deviation with a window of 10 min over the time-series Green Area:
Rotor Area
domain. However, the turbulence parameters of the numerical simulation are not the same
at diﬀerent heights with regard to the measurement data shown in the ﬁgures in Appendix C,
which can cause the diﬀerence at lower heights. Another reason for the diﬀerence is that the
numerical simulation assumes neutral stability, whereas the measurements are performed
at stable conditions. This leads to a diﬀerence in the development of the wake, since less
mixing will occur during atmospheric stable conditions compared to atmospheric neutral
conditions. These will lead to a lower velocity deﬁcit with the numerical simulation in
Ellipsys3D shown in Figure 4.5. Furthermore, the diﬀerence could be related to the statistics
as only one turbulence seed was used for a short time period. In addition, the discrepancy
between the turbulence intensity at 32 m can be due to the determination of the wavelength,
the ﬁltering, the duration of the numerical simulation and the turbulence seed.
4.1.3
Wake Deﬂection
The deﬂection of the wake was determined with the numerical simulations, the FLORIS
model and the measurement data. At ﬁrst the wake center obtained from the measurement
data will be discussed in comparison to the FLORIS model. This will be followed by the
analysis of the wake deﬂection determined with the numerical simulation in Ellipsys3D.
Figure 4.9 shows the relation of the wake center with respect to yaw angle at the SWiFT
facility. The wake center was determined using the Gaussian Fit horizontally at hub height,
which is described into more detail in Appendix A. However, a yaw misalignment was

As expected, Figure 4.9 indicates that the deﬂection increases at higher yaw angles. The
dependency of the wake with regard to the wind speed could not be analyzed due to the
lack of data. It is expected that the deﬂection is larger at lower wind speeds compared
to higher wind speeds. This is due to the thrust coeﬃcient shown in Figure 3.11, which
is higher at lower wind speeds. With a higher thrust, there will be a higher lateral force
present which induces a higher lateral velocity described in Section 2.1.3. In the FLORIS
model Equation 2.18 is used to calculate the wake deﬂection.
The deﬂection of the wake obtained with the numerical simulations is shown in Figure 4.10
for ψ1 = [-30◦, 0◦, 30◦] respectively. The ﬁgures show the ﬂowﬁeld of
u
u∞at hub height
with a single turbine from the numerical simulation and from the FLORIS model. As men-
tioned in the previous section, the wake center was determined with the Gaussian function.
The wake boundary was determined in a similar manner with a Gaussian function and
is described in Appendix B. Two details can be detected in Figure 4.10: the asymmetric
behaviour of the wake and the velocity deﬁcit. The velocity deﬁcit was examined in Sec-
tion 4.1.2. A possible explanation of the asymmetric behaviour of the wake is discussed in
the next section.
43
==PAGE:67==
The asymmetry in the wake behaviour was detected in Fleming et al. (2014) [29] and was
described in Fleming et al. 2018 [24]. A positive yaw angle leads to a higher deﬂection
compared to a negative yaw angle, which is not captured by the FLORIS model. In the
FLORIS model by Gebraad et al. (2016) [31] this asymmetry was included with the param-
eters ad and bd through a simple linear function [33], shown in Table 6. This was neglected
in this study in order to evaluate the performance of the FLORIS model. Fleming et al.
(2018) [24] reconsiders the origin of the asymmetry and states that the deﬂection is due to
the interaction between the counter-vortex pair, the wake (which rotates in the opposite di-
rection of the blade rotation) and the shear proﬁle. Due to the shear proﬁle of the incoming
wind, the vortex in the upper region of the rotor has a higher rotational strength compared
to the vortex strength in the lower region. The strength in the force changes depending on
the yaw angle. This is due to the change in the periodic loads depending on the azimuth
angle shown in Zalkind et al. (2016) [90]. Depending on the yaw angle and the rotation
of the rotor, the blade will be rotating against the wind or with the wind changing the
relative wind speed and thus the angle of attack. This will result in either an increase in
the upper vortex strength and a decrease in the lower vortex strength or the exact opposite.
Furthermore, in the case of a counter-clockwise rotation (negative), the rotation of the top
vortex aids the rotation of the wake. In the case with a clockwise rotation (positive), the
vortex of the lower vortex aids the rotation of the wake. However, since the wind speed is
lower due to the shear proﬁle, the contribution of the lower vortex is smaller. This can be a
possible cause for the asymmetry of the wake according to Fleming et al. (2018) [24]. This
can also be observed in Figure 4.11, illustrating the v-component within the wake. The
absolute vertical component is v = 0.109 [m/s] at a yaw angle of ψ1 = −30◦at hub height
and at S = 5R in comparison to v = 0.083 [m/s] at a yaw angle of ψ1 = 30◦. Indicating a
larger lateral velocity and, thus, a larger deﬂection for ψ1 = −30◦.

The same wake deﬂections were observed for the cases performed with the V27 turbine and
the SOWFA simulations performed in Fleming et al (2014) for the negative yaw angles.
However, a discrepancy was detected for the deﬂections obtained with the positive yaw
angles.
The SOWFA simulations were performed with the NREL 5-MW wind turbine
at a turbulence intensity of 6.3%, a shear exponent of 0.11 and at neutral stability. In
comparison to a tubulence intensity of 14.3% and a shear exponent of 0.18, which can be a
possible cause for the diﬀerence. The eﬀect of the shear proﬁle and the turbulence intensity
on the deﬂection was investigated in Bart et al. [6] through a wind tunnel experiment. At a
higher turbulence intensity the wake recovery was higher resulting in a lower velocity deﬁcit
and the deﬂection seemed to be similar compared to lower turbulence intensity. Vollmer
et al. (2016) [86] investigated the inﬂuence of the atmospheric stability through an LES
study and concluded that a stronger deﬂection is present during stable cases, than during
neutral cases. This is advantageous since the wake losses are the highest during stable
cases. During unstable cases no correlation was found between the wake deﬂection and the
yaw angle. The curling behaviour of the wake due to the counter-vortex pair, described
in various papers [6] [86] [9], was observed in Figure 4.16 and will be elaborated on in
Section 4.2.

4.1.4
Wake Boundary
The wake boundary was determined based on a Gaussian ﬁt described in Appendix B and
was applied to the numerical simulations, the FLORIS model and the measurement data. A
Gaussian ﬁt was used to determine the boundary since it was not possible to determine the
boundary of the wake within the temporal domain due to the continuously changing yaw
heading. Furthermore, the same approach was used by Niayifar, Amin [61] & Porte-Agel
and Fleming et al. [29].
The wake boundary for the measurement data was determined by averaging the Lidar scans
