reaches the limit of the Lidar scan. Furthermore, it is noticeable that the FLORIS model is
symmetric. This is related to the deﬁnition of wake growth in the FLORIS model, depicted
by parameters kz and ky shown in Section 2.3. These parameters are currently identical.
The wake boundary obtained from the numerical simulations has an elliptical shape. This
can be due to the diﬀerence in the turbulence kinetic energy at diﬀerent height, described
in the previous section. This results in a diﬀerence of the mixing between the ambient air
and the wake at diﬀerent heights. It could also be related to the average of the Lidar scans.
4.1.5
Near Wake Length
As described in Section 2.1.1 the wake can be modelled with a double Gaussian distribution
in the near wake and single Gaussian distribution in the far wake [45]. In order, to determine
the near wake length of the simulations the method described in Sørensen et al. (2015) [70]
was used. There the onset of the far wake region was determined by ﬁtting a single peak
Gaussian distribution until a correlation coeﬃcient of 0.99 was achieved by looking at
a relative velocity below 0.9 u
u∞. This provided the possibility to determine to tune the
FLORIS model, described in Appendix D.

height)
The dependency of the near wake region and the yaw angle is illustrated in Figure 4.14,
indicating the Gaussian distribution at the beginning of the far wake region. The ﬁgure also
shows that the near wake region ranges between S = ≈[5.6R, 5.8R]. It is also noticeable that
the term x0
R is smaller at large yaw angles, indicating that the wake breaks down faster at
larger yaw angles. This could be due to the instability of the wake when the rotor is yawed.
This could also be due to the larger lateral velocity described in Section 4.1.3, breaking
down the near wake region faster.
This was also observed in Medici & Alfredson [54],
stating that there is a faster recovery of the velocity deﬁcit due to higher mixing and a
shorter persistence of the tip vortices.
4.2
Two Turbines
Following the previous section, this section will continue describing the complexity of the
wake within a wind farm. This will be done by investigating the turbulence within the wake
and the velocity deﬁcit with multiple turbines in a row. The analyzes will be conducted
through the use of the numerical simulations described in Section 2.2 and the FLORIS
model described in Section 2.3. The aim is to provide a solid understanding of the wake
during a yawed conﬁguration within the wind farm.
The velocity deﬁcit of four wind turbines in a row is shown in Figure 4.15 with the FLORIS
model and the numerical simulation. Furthermore, it can be observed that the velocity
deﬁcit reaches the same minimum after the second, the third and the fourth turbine with
ψ1 = 0[◦] with LES. The incoming velocity is higher with ψ1 = 30[◦] for the second, third
and fourth turbine, indicating that steering the wake is not only beneﬁcial for the second
49
==PAGE:73==
0

30

Figure 4.15: Comparison between the relative velocity
U
U∞at ψ = [0◦, 0◦, 0◦, 0◦] and ψ = [−30◦, 0◦, 0◦, 0◦] between
the numerical simulation and the FLORIS model with a single turbine (U∞is the incoming horizontal wind speed
at hub height)
turbine, but also for the entire row. The power gain of each turbine within the row is
illustrated in Section 5.
Figure 4.16 illustrates the vertical scan of the horizontal velocity
u
u∞at S = 5R and at S =
15R, which is behind the ﬁrst and the second turbine respectively. At a downstream distance
of 5R and ψ1 = −30[◦], the wake follows a kidney shape, which is due to the counter vortex
pair described in section 2.1.4. It is interesting to note that the wake is deﬂected after the
second turbine with ψ2 = 0[◦]. As mentioned in Section 2.1, the main cause of the deﬂection
is due to a lateral velocity component. Since the deﬂected wake of the ﬁrst turbine partially
hits the second turbine, an azimuthal variation in the turbine loading is created. This would
create the same eﬀect as yawing the wind turbine, leading to a higher axial induction in
the "downwind" region and a lower axial induction in the "upwind" region as described in
Section 2.1.2. In this case, a higher induction will be present in the rotor region within the
wake and a lower induction will be present in the rotor region outside of the wake. The
deﬂection of the second wind turbine has the same deﬂection, if the upstream turbine had
a yaw angle of ψ1 = −15◦. In Fleming et al. (2018) [24] this is referred to as "secondary
steering". This study also found that the FLORIS model is not able to predict "secondary
steering" since the wake combination model is based on the sum of squares, which showed
an error in prediction compared to newer methods according to Trabucchi et al. (2017) [79].
50
==PAGE:74==

Figure 4.16: Vertical scan of the normalized streamwise velocity deﬁcit ∆u
u∞at S = 5R and S = 15R. Viewpoint from
downstream position. R2 indicates the goodness of the wake boundary determination. Red Points: Indicate the
wake boundary. Black Point : Indicates the wake center. Black line: Indicates the rotor area (u∞is the incoming
streamwise component at hub height)
A ring of high turbulence levels is observed behind the rotor. This was also determined in
the studies conducted by Bartl et al. (2017) [7] and in Eriksen et al. (2017) [22]. Within this
turbulence ring, the tip vortices decay into turbulence structures. The further downstream,
the ring decreases in magnitude and blurs out to the surroundings [6]. In Figure 4.17 the
turbulence ring is clearly visible for the case of ψ1 = 0[◦] at S = 5R. The ring slowly
blurs out at S = 15R. In addition, it shows that the area of low turbulence within the ring
becomes smaller and increases in magnitude. In Bartl et al. (2018) [6] it was observed that
the highest turbulent kinetic energy was found at the location of the highest gradient in
the streamwise velocity. In addition, it is noticeable how the ring is deﬂected and deformed
at ψ1 = −30[◦]. The strength of the turbulence was not uniform along the ring. This is
due to the loading variation within the blade (described in Section 2.1), which experiences
a higher thrust at the "upwind" region compared to the "downwind" region. Moreover,
the thrust coeﬃcient reduces at larger yaw angles reducing the strength of the vortices. It
can also be noticed that in the case with ψ1 = 0[◦] and ψ1 = −30[◦], at 5R and 15R, the
turbulence kinetic energy is higher at higher heights compared to lower heights. This is
due to the shear proﬁle; the turbulence is stronger at the upper part than in the lower part
shown in Sanderse et al. (2009) [67].

In the cases where the second turbine is present at S = 10R, it can be observed that the
turbulence ring is present again with higher turbulence levels in its surroundings due to the
wake of the ﬁrst turbine. Bartl et al. (2018) [6] identiﬁed that at higher turbulence levels in
the ambient air reduces the peaks within the turbulence rings due to an increased mixing.
Knowing the turbulence levels within the wake is important for yaw control studies since
the fatigue loads increases at cases with a higher turbulence level.
52
==PAGE:76==
4.3
Wake Meandering
This section describes the meandering eﬀect of the wake at each downstream distance and
investigates how the meandering eﬀects diﬀer relative to the yaw angle. The analysis focuses
on the wake meandering eﬀects due to a singular turbine with undisturbed inﬂow condition.
The dynamic behaviour of the wake is often denoted as wake meandering and its under-
standing is critical as it increases the fatigue loads on a downstream turbine. In Medici &
Alfredson (2006) [54] it was argued that the low-frequency shedding might cause the wake
to meander. Their study was conducted with a two-bladed turbine with uniform inﬂow
conditions. Larsen et al. (2008) [48] described the process as a series of consecutive wake
releases, which propagate downstream due to the mean ﬂowﬁeld and are displaced within a
plane due to large scale turbulence structures. Due to the large scale turbulence structures,
the wind turbine experiences partial wake alignment. This introduces higher diﬀerential
loads. The large scale turbulence structure also enhances the turbulence mixing.

described in Appendix A. As the wake did not follow a Gaussian distribution at far wake
conditions at each instantaneous time-stamp, the Gaussian ﬁt could not be used, shown in
Figure 4.21. Figure 4.18 shows how the meandering eﬀects increases further downstream.
This was detected in Coudou et al. (2018) [17], where an increase in the meandering am-
plitude was detected as function of space. Furthermore, it can be seen how the wake is
deﬂected when the turbine is yawed. There it can be seen that the wake center obtained
from the mean vertical scan correlates with the median obtained from the instantaneous
wake center until a downstream distance of 8R-9R. After that, the mean wake center and
the median wake center diverge. This indicates that determining the wake center instan-

The relation of the wake meandering at diﬀerent yaw angles is depicted in Figure 4.19 and
Table 11. Since only one turbulence seed was used for the simulation, no deﬁnitive conclu-
sion can be drawn with regard to the wake meandering and the yaw angle. In Medici &
Alfredson [54], the meandering eﬀects of a wind turbine in yaw was studied. There it was
noticed that the shedding frequency increased at larger yaw angles.
In addition, it was also noticed that there was a low correlation between the thrust and
the instantaneous wake center. The correlation was determined using the Matlab function
’xcorr’ 8, from which the absolute maximum correlation coeﬃcient between the two time-
series was selected.
The low correlation indicates that it is diﬃcult to steer the wake
center instantaneously by yawing the turbine. Furthermore, this indicates that the eﬀect
of yaw steering is more beneﬁcial for longer time periods.
This analysis highlights the
need for further investigation regarding the eﬀect of the yaw angle on the wake meandering
phenomena.

4.4
Summary
In this chapter, the dynamics of the wake was described into detail. It provides an overview
of the wake characteristic obtained from the full scale ﬁeld test by describing the behaviour
of the power output and the wake center with respect to the yaw angle. This is followed
by a comparison between the velocity deﬁcit of the SWiFT facility, the FLORIS model and
the numerical simulation in Ellipsys3D. During the comparison a discrepancy between each
method was detected. The FLORIS model is not capable to accurately predict the velocity
deﬁcit at near wake regions. The velocity deﬁcit between the numerical simulation and the
SWiFT facility is due to the diﬀerence in the stability, the atmospheric turbulence param-
eters and that a single turbulence seed was used. The atmospheric turbulence parameters
at 32 m was used as input for the numerical simulation. However, these parameters vary
with height which can not be incorporated in the numerical simulation. Furthermore, the
discrepancy in the velocity deﬁcit is also related due to the diﬀerence in the atmospheric
stability. The numerical simulation assumes neutral condition, whereas stable condition is
present at the SWiFT facility. This results in a diﬀerent development of the wake in the
numerical simulation compared to the SWiFT facility.
Furthermore, the density between the numerical simulation and the SWiFT facility have
diﬀerent magnitudes, namely 1.225
kg
m3 and 1.12
kg
m3 . This will lead to a diﬀerence in the
power and the thrust of 9%, between the measurement data and the FLORIS model. How-
ever, this will not alter the conclusion of the turbine response since it is based on a relative
comparison.
After the comparison with the SWIFT facility, the performance between the numerical sim-
ulation and the FLORIS simulation was compared. During the comparison an asymmetry
was detected in the wake deﬂection, which is related to the interaction between the shear
proﬁle, the counter vortex pair and the rotation of the wake. The asymmetry of the wake
was not captured by the FLORIS model. Furthermore, a diﬀerence was detected with the
velocity deﬁcit obtained by the FLORIS model and the numerical simulation. This is since
the FLORIS model, does not model the induction and uses an assumption for the near
wake region.
The wake boundary of the numerical simulation, the FLORIS model and the measurement
data were also compared. There a discrepancy was detected again. The wake boundary of
the FLORIS model is extremely symmetric as the wake growth is similar lateral and vertical.
The wake boundary of the measurement data is roughly symmetric, but at a larger yaw an-
gle the wake boundary exceeds the limit of the Lidar. The wake boundary of the numerical
simulation is not symmetric. This can be due to the diﬀerence in the turbulence parameters.
The wake behaviour behind the second wind turbine was analyzed with regard to the veloc-
56
==PAGE:80==
ity deﬁcit and the turbulence level. It demonstrated that the turbulence level is inﬂuenced
by the yaw angle of the turbine and the wake of the upstream turbine. Furthermore, a
deﬂection of the wake of the second turbine was visible when the ﬁrst turbine had a large
yaw angle.
Finally, the meandering of the wake was analyzed at diﬀerent yaw angles. The analysis
showed that the instantaneous wake center and the mean wake center diﬀer with each other.
It also showed that there was a low correlation between the thrust and the instantaneous
wake center.
57
==PAGE:81==
5
Turbine Response
This chapter focuses on the response of the turbine and the wind turbine components
during yaw control with the case study described in Section 3. The analysis will focus
on the upstream and downstream turbine and determine whether the fatigue load can
be alleviated. Thomson & Sorensen (1999) [78] determined that an increase of turbulence
increases the fatigue loads up to 15% with respect to the ambient inﬂow conditions. Kragh &
Hansen (2014) [47] and Zalkind & Pao (2016) [90], determined a decrease in the blade loads
for certain yaw angles, whereas an increase at other components, such as the drive-train
and the tower, were observed. However, Damian et al. (2017) [18] concludes, in contrast
to other studies, that the fatigue loads do not necessarily decrease with increasing yaw
angles. The turbine response will be determined with the aerolastic tool Flex5, developed
at the Technical University of Denmark by Stig Øye for a single turbine and two turbines.
This was done by extracting the ﬂowﬁeld of the numerical simulation at a certain distance
and use it as an input for Flex5. The turbine was then yawed in Flex5 to determine the
DEL. This was described in Chapter 3. Knowing the behaviour of the wake described in
Chapter 4 provides a better understanding of the relation of the wake position relative to
the rotor area and, thus, the relation between the yaw angle and the equivalent loads. This
provides the opportunity to determine the driving factors inﬂuencing the fatigue loads. The
conﬁguration with four turbines in a row will be used to determine the total power output
with a changing yaw angle in the ﬁrst turbine. The last chapter 6 focuses on connecting the
behaviour of the wake and the response of the turbine to determine the optimal operation
condition. This paves the path to develop a cost model to improve the power output of the
wind farm including the eﬀect on the fatigue loads.
5.1
Single Turbine
This section will describe the turbine response of a single turbine with respect to diﬀerent
yaw conﬁgurations. It will focus on the DEL on the wind turbine components, which is de-
termined using the rainﬂow counting method described in Section 3.4.2. The power output
of the upstream turbine will not be discussed here since it has a similar behaviour shown in
Figure 3.9. The power reduces with an increasing yaw angle. As mentioned in Section 2.2,
the numerical simulations were performed in Ellipsys3D and the turbine was modeled with
the actuator line model, in order to simulate the wake characteristics downstream. This will
be referred to as "LES turbines" during the analysis. Furthermore, the aerolastic behaviour
of the turbine was determined by extracting the ﬂowﬁeld at a certain downstream distance
and use it as an input for the aerolastic tool FLEX5. These turbines will be referred to
as "ghost turbines", since it is not physically interacting with the ﬂuid in the numerical
domain. Chapter 3 gives an overview of the simulated cases.

Figure 5.1: Azimuthal loading of the blade at uniform inﬂow condition and shear inﬂow condition using the unsteady
BEM code. Flowﬁeld at S = 0R of the simulation case with no turbines was used for the case with a shear inﬂow
condition. Calculations were performed with no tilt angle in order to exclude the azimuthal variation due to the tilt
angle on the blade loads. The local blade loads were normalized with the local blade load with no yaw angle and
uniform inﬂow condition.
As described in Section 2.1.2, the blade will experience an azimuthal variation on the load-
ing due to the yaw-misalignment. This is shown in Figure 5.1, illustrating the thrust relative
to the azimuth angle at diﬀerent yaw angles. It can be seen that the amplitude decreases or
increases at a positive yaw angle or a negative yaw angle compared to no yaw angle. This
is described in Zalkind et al.
[90] stating that the advancing and retreating behaviour of
the blade with respect to the crosswind creates a change in the angle of attack, lift forces
and drag forces. This is visualized in Figure 5.3, showing the change in the AOA. In order
to illustrate the advancing and retracting behaviour of the blade, the rotational velocity
of the incoming wind is shown in Figure 5.2 with uniform inﬂow condition and with shear
inﬂow condition at ψ1 = [−30◦, 0◦, 30◦]. Due to the shear layer and the yaw angle the
behaviour of the blade diﬀers, which can be seen in the rotational velocity component of
the incoming wind speed. The rotational velocity is the largest at 120◦and at 300◦for
ψ1 = −30◦and ψ1 = 30◦respectively for a shear inﬂow condition. The change in the angle
of attack and the blade loading is shown in Figure 5.3 , determined with the unsteady BEM
code described in Appendix E. Here, the variation due to the yaw angle and the shear angle
can be clearly detected.
Since the DEL depends on the cyclic occurrence of the loads, the advancing and retreating
motion of the blade will inﬂuence the fatigue loading on the components of the wind turbine.
This is shown in Figure 5.4, where the fatigue loads of the wind turbine components with
respect to the yaw angle is illustrated. The standalone FLEX5 tool is based on the unsteady
BEM code (described in Appendix E). There the incoming wind speed at a certain position is
used and it does not vary if the blade is deﬂected. Furthermore, as mentioned in Appendix D
care needs to be taken when analyzing the loads of the turbine at large yaw angles based
on the BEM method due to the implemented yaw model.

61
==PAGE:85==
The fatigue loads obtained from the standalone FLEX5 tool with the extracted velocity at
S = 0R of the simulation case with no turbines is shown in Figure 5.4 for the ﬂapwise bend-
ing moment (FlapM), the edgewise bending moment (EdgeM), the tower bottom bending
moment in the x-axis (TBBM MX) and the tower bottom bending moment in the y-axis
(TBBM MY).
• Flapwise Bending Moment: In Damiani et al. (2018) [18] similar results were obtained
for the distribution of the DEL of the ﬂapwise bending moment. The increase of the
ﬂapwise short-term equivalent load due to the yaw angle is related to the increase of
the amplitude of the blade loading at large yaw angles, shown in Figure 5.1.
• Edgewise Bending Moment: Similar to the results from Damiani et al. (2018) [18] the
fatigue loads due to the edgewise bending moment remains almost similar and a small
diﬀerence can be seen between a positive yaw angle and a negative yaw angle. The
main inﬂuence to the fatigue load of the edgewise bending moment is gravity and the
oscillation of the thrust component acting in the rotor plane. This indicates that the
amplitude of the oscillation of the thrust component is slightly lower at negative yaw
compared to positive yaw. This can be due to the variation of the azimuthal loading
across the rotor, described in Section 2.1.2, and the rotation of the blade. The local
thrust at a blade segment enhances or reduces the amplitude of the thrust component
in the direction of the edgewise movement.
• Tower bottom around the y-axis: The DEL due to the tower bending moment in
the y-axis reduces at large yaw angles with the standalone FLEX5 tool. The fore-aft
motion of the tower is related to the thrust which reduces at large yaw angles. This
will also lead to a reduction of the moment around the y-axis.
• Tower bottom around the x-axis: The DEL due to the tower bending moment in the
x-axis depends on the yaw moment. Due to diﬀerences within the incoming ﬂowﬁeld
the rotor creates a yaw moment. A rotor in yaw enhances the azimuthal diﬀerences
across the rotor, but reduces the magnitude of the thrust as well. This leads to a high
DEL at ψ1 = 0◦and a reduction at large yaw angles.
• Tower bottom around the z-axis: The DEL in the tower bottom around the z-axis is
mainly inﬂuenced by the side-side motion of the turbine. Depending on the location
of the load sensor, the tower will be either in tension or in compression depending on
the yaw angle. In Figure 5.4, the DEL increases for positive yaw angles and decreases
slightly for negative yaw angles. This indicates that the load sensor is located at a
position in the tower where the tower experiences an increase in the loads due to a
positive yaw angle.
In Ennis et al. (2018) [21] the fatigue loads on the V27 on the SWiFT facility have been
analyzed. This was done for the upstream turbine, WTGa1 in Figure 3.1. There it was
62
==PAGE:86==
observed that ﬂapwise DEL is 3-5 times larger than the edgewise DEL. Furthermore, it was
noticed that the DEL of the ﬂapwise bending moment is higher at positive yaw angles in
comparison to negative yaw angles. This is in contradiction with Figure 5.4. However, this
can be related due to the low turbulence intensity (TI ≈0.07 [-]) and the high wind shear
(α ≈0.4 [-]). This indicates that determining the wind shear and turbulence intensity is
crucial for wind farm control with regard to the fatigue loading.
Furthermore, Fleming et al. (2014) [29] the power output and the ﬂapwise bending moment
of the upstream turbine was evaluated with the NREL5MW turbine. The comparison is
shown in Table 15 in Appendix H. There it can be seen that the power output obtained with
the simulations conducted by Fleming has a similar trend to the power output obtained
with the V27. However, a diﬀerent trend is visible for the ﬂapwise bending moment. This
can be related to the diﬀerence in the turbine model. The V27 is an old wind turbine and is
stiﬀer compared to the NREL5MW wind turbine. Furthermore, diﬀerent inﬂow conditions
were also applicable.
5.2
Two Turbines
This section describes the turbine response at the downstream wind turbine. This was done
with the numerical cases tabulated in Table 5 in Chapter 3. For each numerical simulation
the ﬂowﬁeld was extracted at each downstream distance and used as an input for Flex5.
As described in Section 4, a higher yaw angle will lead to a larger deﬂection and the wake
will have a kidney shape at very large yaw angles. It was also shown that the wake has an
asymmetric behaviour and that the turbulence level is altered when the turbine is yawed.
These changes in the velocity deﬁcit and the turbulence will eﬀect the power output and
the fatigue loads of the downstream turbine. The power output at various yaw angles for
downstream wind turbine will be analyzed. This will be followed by the analyzes of the
DEL of the downstream wind turbine at various spacing.
5.2.1
Power Output
Figure 5.5 illustrates the cumulative power output obtained with the numerical simula-
tion and the FLORIS model, normalized with the power of the upstream turbine with
no yaw-misalignment. The power output corresponds with the velocity deﬁcit illustrated
in Figure 4.5. This correspondence is expected since the power has a cubic relationship
with the velocity. The power output at ψ1 = −30◦and ψ1 = 30◦are lower compared to
ψ1 = 0◦due to the power loss of the upstream turbine at ±30◦. Furthermore, the eﬀect of
deﬂecting the wake can be seen in the comparison between the power output at ψ1 = −30◦
and ψ1 = 30◦. At ψ1 = −30◦the wake is deﬂected further away, which results into a
higher power output for the downstream wind turbine at a closer spacing in comparison to
ψ1 = 30◦. This eﬀect is not visible in the power output obtained from the FLORIS model,

the power output between S = [5R, 10R] is higher in the FLORIS model compared to the
numerical simulation due to the diﬀerence in the velocity deﬁcit illustrated in Figure 4.5.
5.2.2
Fatigue Loads
The DEL of the downstream turbine with respect to the yaw angle and the downstream
distance is illustrated in Figure 5.6 to Figure 5.9. The analysis will ﬁrst focus on the eﬀect
of the wake on the DEL. This is done by looking at the diﬀerence between the DEL of the
upstream turbine, shown in Figure 5.4, and the DEL within the wake. This is followed by
the analysis of the total DEL. The DEL is normalized for each wind turbine component of
the downstream turbine with the upstream turbine at at S = 0R with ψ1 = 0◦. The DEL
was determined by extracting the ﬂowﬁeld at each downstream distance and was used as
an input to the aerolastic tool Flex5. These data points will be used in Section 6 to build a
surrogate model to evaluate the power gain with regard to the fatigue loads. Comparable

the inﬂuence of the wake.

higher compared to ψ1 = 30◦at S >6R & S < 8R. This is due the partial wake eﬀect. At
ψ1 = −30◦the wake deﬂects more, which means that the turbine will experience a partial
wake at closer distances compared to ψ1 = 30◦. The partial wake eﬀect at ψ1 = 30◦occurs
between S > 8R & S < 11R as the wake is deﬂected less. Furthermore, Figure 5.6 illustrates
the total normalized DEL, which includes the eﬀect due to the yaw angle ψ2 and the wake
deﬂection due to the upstream turbine. The DEL of the ﬂapwise bending moment shows
a symmetric behaviour at ψ1 = 0◦. At large yaw angles of the downstream turbine (±ψ2)
the fatigue loads increase at each downstream distance corresponding with Figure 5.4. At
ψ2 = 0◦the DEL increases up to S ≈10R and then decreases, due to the increase and
decrease of the turbulent structures further downstream. Furthermore, the DEL increases
at ψ1 = −30◦and at ψ2 > 0◦since the rotor is oriented towards the deﬂected wake. The
same occurs with ψ1 = 30◦and at ψ2 < 0◦.

Figure 5.8: DEL loads of the tower bending moment around the y-axis (For-Aft motion) were determined with Flex5
normalized with the DEL of the upstream turbine at S = 0R with ψ1 = 0◦. a, b, c: DEL at ψ1 = [−30◦, 0◦, 30◦]

Diﬀerence between the DEL of the

The DEL of the tower bottom bending moment around the y-axis mainly depends on the
fore-aft motion of the turbine. It is inﬂuenced by the mean velocity within the wake and
the turbulence structures. Figure 5.8 shows the eﬀect only due to the wake and the total
DEL. At ψ1 = 0◦the DEL increases up to S≈10R, due to the increase of the velocity and
the large turbulence levels. After S≈16R, the DEL decreases as the turbulence structures
within the wake decreases. At ψ1 = −30◦the wake has the highest deﬂection, indicating
that eﬀect due to the turbulence structure on the DEL is reduced.
However, a larger
deﬂection indicates a faster recovery of the velocity deﬁcit. This is visible in the DEL,
where a high DEL is obtained between S≈R6-R8 since the velocity in the wake is higher in
comparison to ψ1 = 0◦and ψ1 = 30◦. The eﬀect of the wake at ψ1 = 30◦is more dominant,
resulting in a high DEL starting at S≈8R. The eﬀect of the wake is higher in comparison to
the case with no yaw due to the velocity deﬁcit and the turbulence structures. This results
in a higher DEL at ψ1 = 30◦between 6R < S < 8R in comparison to ψ1 = 0◦. A reduction
of the DEL at large yaw angles is observed, which corresponding to Figure 5.4.

(f) TBBM MZ, ψ1 = 30◦
Figure 5.9: DEL loads of the tower bending moment around the z-axis (Side-Side motion) were determined with Flex5
normalized with the DEL of the upstream turbine at S = 0R with ψ1 = 0◦. a, b, c: DEL at ψ1 = [−30◦, 0◦, 30◦]
with diﬀerent spacing and yaw angles for the downstream turbine. d, e, f:
Diﬀerence between the DEL of the
upstream and downstream turbine.
The DEL is inﬂuenced by the side-side motion of the turbine. This motion is increased
when the turbine is in the wake resulting in an increase in DEL at ψ2 > 0◦and a decrease
at ψ2 < 0◦due to the location of the sensor at ψ1 = 0◦shown in Figure 5.9. This is similar
to the DEL of the upstream turbine shown in Figure 5.4. A higher DEL is observed at
ψ1 = −30◦and positive yaw angles for the downstream turbine for the DEL from the tower
bottom around the y-axis in comparison to ψ1 = 30◦. This is related to the deﬂection of
the wake, the lateral wind speed and the downstream yaw angle. A yawed rotor will exert
a lateral force which excites the side-side motion. At ψ1 = −30◦& ψ2 > 0◦, the rotor
is oriented towards the deﬂected wake, increasing the later force. This results in a higher
DEL in comparison to ψ1 = 30◦& ψ2 > 0◦, where the rotor is not oriented towards the
deﬂected wake. This also results in a slight reduction of the DEL at ψ1 = −30◦& ψ2 < 0◦,
in comparison to ψ1 = 0◦& ψ2 < 0◦. The DEL increases for ψ1 = 30◦& ψ2 < 0◦since the
rotor is oriented towards the deﬂected wake. The same eﬀect is visible in Figure 5.9 for the
total DEL of the tower bending moment around the y-axis.

Figure 5.10: DEL loads of the tower bending moment around the x-axis were determined with Flex5 normalized
with the DEL of the upstream turbine at S = 0R with ψ1 = 0◦. a, b, c: DEL at ψ1 = [−30◦, 0◦, 30◦] with diﬀerent

Diﬀerence between the DEL of the upstream and
downstream turbine.
Figure 5.10 shows the diﬀerence of the yaw-moment exerted on the tower bottom of the
downstream turbine and the upstream turbine. Due to the diﬀerence within the incoming
ﬂowﬁeld, the rotor creates a yaw moment. A rotor in yaw enhances the azimuthal diﬀerence
across the rotor, but reduces the magnitude of the thrust as well. The DEL is the largest
within the wake at S ≈8R and reduces at large yaw angles. At ψ1 = −30◦the DEL is
the largest at a closer spacing S ≈5R and at ψ2 > 0◦. This is since the wake is deﬂected,
resulting in a partial wake eﬀect creating an azimuthal diﬀerence across the blade and thus
increasing the DEL. In addition, a higher DEL is obtained at ψ2 > 0◦since the rotor is
oriented towards the deﬂected wake increasing the thrust. At ψ1 = 30◦the opposite occurs
at S ≈6R. The DEL is higher at ψ1 = −30◦and ψ1 = 30◦due to the partial wake eﬀect
creating a yaw moment.
69
==PAGE:93==
5.3
Four Turbines in a Row
This section describes the power output of a wind farm which consists of four wind turbines
in a row. This analysis is conducted to showcase that deﬂecting the wake does not only
inﬂuence the power output of the ﬁrst downstream turbine but of all the turbines within a
row. The power output of the "LES" turbines were used for this analysis. The simulation
case is described in more detail in Section 3.2.
Figure 5.11 illustrates the eﬀect of steering the wake of the upstream turbine on the tur-
bines downstream. Here, the case with ψ = 0◦for all turbines will be considered as the
baseline case. In Table 12, it is noticeable that the power output decreases at the baseline
case for each downstream turbine as it is operating within the wake. In the case, with
ψ1 = ±5◦, the power output slowly increases after the second turbine. This could indicate
that the wake breaks down faster due to instabilities and that the velocity deﬁcit recovers
faster in comparison to the base case. The power of the third and the fourth turbine is
almost similar to the case with ψ1 = −30◦. In the latter case, the velocity deﬁcit recovers
faster for the second turbine, resulting in a higher power output for the second turbine.
However, this comes at a cost for a large power loss for the upstream turbine. As mentioned
in Section 4.2, the wake of the downstream turbine is deﬂected as well due to the deﬂected
wake of the upstream turbine, which is referred to as "secondary steering". This eﬀect is
also visible for the wake of the third turbine, resulting in a higher power output for the
third and fourth turbine. This can also be seen in Figure 4.15 which illustrates the velocity
deﬁcit at hub height of four turbines in row in Section 4.2.
Furthermore, the power of the upstream turbine is also eﬀected by the induction of the
downstream turbine, which reduces the power output of the upstream turbine. The dif-
ference of the power output of the upstream turbine with no downstream turbine and a
downstream turbine at S = 10R is -1.3%. More research will be required to determine the
eﬀect of the induction zone of the downstream turbine during yaw based operation.

==PAGE:95==
5.4
Summary
This chapter provided an overview of the response of the upstream and the downstream
turbine with respect to the yaw angle of the upstream and the downstream wind turbine.
It illustrated the eﬀect of the yaw angle on the azimuthal variation of the AOA and the
local loading on each blade segment. The analysis of the turbine response focused on the
upstream turbine and the downstream turbine at diﬀerent yaw angles. It was conducted
by investigating the "ghost" turbines which do not interact with the ﬂuid in the numerical
domain in Ellipsys3D. At ﬁrst, the upstream wind turbine was analyzed. The relation be-
tween the DEL and ψ1 due to the ﬂapwise bending moment, the edgewise bending moment
and the tower bending moment around each axis was investigated. It showed that the DEL
due to the ﬂapwise bending moment increased for large yaw angles. The DEL, due to the
tower bending moment around the x-axis and the y-axis, reduced at large yaw angles. The
DEL of the edgewise bending moment and the tower bending moment around the z-axis
showed a reduction of the DEL at ψ1 < 0◦and an increase at ψ1 > 0◦.
This is followed by the analysis of the power output for the downstream turbine. The re-
sults were compared with the power output of the FLORIS model, where it showcased that
the FLORIS model is not capable to accurately predict the power output within the near
wake region. In addition, it was also noticed that the power output was similar for positive
and negative yaw angles as the FLORIS model is not capable to predict the asymmetry of
the wake.
After analyzing the power output of the downstream turbine the equivalent loads of the
downstream turbine was investigated with regard to the yaw angle of the upstream and
the downstream wind turbine. There it showed that the DEL is dependent on the velocity
deﬁcit and the turbulence structures within the wake. Due to the deﬂection of the wake a
diﬀerent DEL was determined in comparison to no deﬂection of the wake. This could result
to an increase or a decrease of the DEL. In addition, the eﬀect of partial wake loading was
noticeable.
In the last section, the power output of four turbines in a row was analyzed. During the
analysis it was observed that a power gain was achieved for the second, third and fourth
wind turbine. This indicates that the power gain is not only beneﬁcial for the second tur-
bine. However, a large yaw angle comes at a cost of a reduction of the power output.
After determining the behaviour of the wake in Chapter 4 and it’s inﬂuence on the turbine
response a model was developed that analyzes the impact of yaw control on the power
output and the loading. This was done in Chapter 6.
72
==PAGE:96==
6
Yaw-Based Wind Farm Control
This chapter explains the approach used to develop a model that analyzes the impact
of yaw control on the power output and the loading, which is essential to determine the
potential of a yaw based wind farm control. This was done by using the polynomial chaos
expansion, illustrated in Section 2.4. Current studies have only implemented the power gain
in the cost model (Gebraad et al. (2016) [31]). Van Dijk et al. (2016) [84] has developed
a computational framework based on the FLORIS model and CCBlade, to analyze the
impact of yaw control on the power output and the loading. The results demonstrate that
a decrease in loading is possible at the expense of the power production. This chapter
explains the procedure conducted to use the obtained data from the aerolastic tool Flex5
to create a model which combines the fatigue loads and the power output. Section 2.4
describes the theory of the polynomial chaos expansion and the method used to build the
model, which was done with "chaospy" in Python. The evaluation of the performance of
diﬀerent models is described in Section 6.1. A separate model was created for the power
output, the ﬂapwise bending moment and the tower bending moment around the y-axis for
the upstream and downstream turbine. This was done in order to simplify the model and to
include the most important components inﬂuenced by yaw steering. The edgewise bending
moment was not included since it is heavily inﬂuenced by the gravity. The fatigue load of
the tower bottom bending moment around the z-axis is not included since the fatigue load is
smaller compared to the fatigue load around the y-axis. For the upstream turbine the DEL
of the tower bending moment around the y-axis is 10times larger than the DEL of the tower
bending moment around the z-axis. These models were evaluated in comparison to each
other and to the numerical results. A validation case was simulated in Ellipsys3D to assess
the performance of the model. This is followed by determining the optimal conﬁguration
to steer the wake at each downstream with regard to the power output and the loading.
6.1
Model Selection
This section describes the evaluation of the diﬀerent models build, using the polynomial
chaos expansion described in Section 2.4. Diﬀerent models with diﬀerent orders were build
for the power output and the equivalent loads. The evaluation was conducted by deter-
mining the performance of the model at a downstream distance of S = 5R and S = 14R.
Furthermore, the response of the model in relation to the yaw angle of the upstream and
the downstream turbine was examined.
6.1.1
Model for the Upstream Turbine
As mentioned in Section 3.5, the PCE model for the upstream turbine was only dependant
on the variable ψ1. The relation illustrated in Equation 3.1 was used to model the power
output of the upstream turbine. Figure 6.1 shows the response surface with an order of p

= [3, 4, 5, 6] and the corresponding equivalent loads of the Flex5 simulations for which a
moving average was performed described in Section 3.5. An order of p = 2 was not used
since the equivalent loads of the ﬂapwise bending moment and the tower bending moment
are not symmetric. The PCE model with an order of p = 6 for the DEL of the ﬂapwise
bending moment shows signs of over ﬁtting. Furthermore, at large yaw angles (ψ1) the
slope of the PCE model with an order of p = 4 & p = 5 reduces. This is not the trend
that is expected with increasing yaw angle, which pinpoints the selection for the model to
an order of p = 3 to develop the ﬁnal model. For the tower bending moment around the
y-axis no signiﬁcant diﬀerence was seen between the PCE models. This resulted in the
PCE model of p = 3, due to its simplicity and faster computational time.
6.1.2
Model for the Downstream Turbine
Figure 6.2 illustrates the coeﬃcients for the models, modelling the power output and the
equivalent loads with a magnitude larger than 4. The ﬁgure shows how the coeﬃcient change
with an increasing order of each PCE model. For the power output of the downstream
turbine, the coeﬃcients for the variables S, S2 and S3 are the largest, indicating that the
power is highly inﬂuenced by the distance. This is to be expected as the power increases
further downstream shown in Figure 5.5. Furthermore, it is noticeable that the variable ψ1
and ψ2 are for each model only visible together with the variable S. This indicates that the
relation between ψ1 & ψ2 is interconnected with the distance. In addition the magnitude
of the coeﬃcients related to the yaw angle increases in magnitude with a larger polynomial
order.
This indicates that the inﬂuence of the yaw angles increases at a higher order.
Furthermore, the magnitude of the coeﬃcients related to the distance are always higher in
comparison to the coeﬃcients related to the yaw angles. This shows that the distance has
74
==PAGE:98==
a larger inﬂuence than the yaw angles of the upstream and downstream turbine. The same
behaviour is also detected with the response surface for the equivalent load of the ﬂapwise
bending moment and for the tower bending moment around the y-axis of the downstream
turbine. A larger inﬂuence due to the spacing than due to the yaw angles.

The response surface was evaluated by comparison with the numerical results obtained in
Section 5.2. Figure 6.3 illustrates the response of the surface with diﬀerent polynomial
orders at a downstream distance of S = 5R and S = 14R. These distances were chosen
since the highest power output was obtained at S = 5R, which will be shown in Section 6.2.
Furthermore, in Fleming et al. (2013) [28] and in Gebraad et al. (2016) [31] a numerical
simulation was performed with two NREL5MW turbines in a row with S = 14R spacing
which gives the opportunity to compare the model and the results.
• Power Output: It can be seen that the polynomial order of p = 6 is over ﬁtting
between −30◦≤ψ1 ≤−15◦at 5R & 14R and with p = 5 between 15◦≤ψ1 ≤30◦at
14R. Showing that both PCE models are over ﬁtting.
• Flapwise Bending Moment: At an order of of p = 6 the model also over-ﬁtted at
−30◦≤ψ1 ≤−15◦at S = 5R and with p = 4 between −30◦≤ψ1 ≤−10◦at S =
5R. At S = 14R the DEL are spread resulting in the oscillating behaviour of the PCE
model with an order of p = [4, 5, 6].
• Tower Bending Moment MY: The PCE model with an order of p = 3 overestimates
the DEL at ψ1 = 0◦at S = 5R. The models with an order of p = [4, 5, 6] capture the
behaviour of the model at that distance. At S = 14R the models have a oscillating
behaviour due to the spread of the DEL. The PCE with an order of p = 3 does not
have this behaviour.
The results from Fleming et al. (2013) [28] and in Gebraad et al. (2016) [31] show a similar
trend for the power output of the downstream turbine which increases at a large yaw angle
of the upstream wind turbine. However, the trend for the DEL of the ﬂapwise bending
moment for the downstream turbine is diﬀerent.
The diﬀerence is due to the diﬀerent
turbine models and the diﬀerent inﬂow conditions. The V27 wind turbine is an old turbine
and is much stiﬀer compared to the NREL5MW turbine. In addition, the shear proﬁle has
a large inﬂuence on the DEL. The contour plots of the DEL at each downstream distance
at ψ1 = [30◦, 0◦, 30◦] and at ψ2 = [−30◦: 2.5◦: 30◦] for the ﬂapwise bending moment, the
edgewise bending moment and the tower bending moment around the y-axis is illustrated
in Appendix J.

The relative error for the power output and the equivalent loads for the downstream turbine
are shown in Figure 6.6, Figure 6.8 and Figure 6.7 respectively. In Figure 6.6, it can be
seen that the relative error of the power output is the highest at a close spacing for each
model, which is within the near wake region (up to ≈6R) of the upstream turbine. At
a larger spacing, the relative error reduces, indicating that the models performs better.
Furthermore, it can be seen that the spread of the relative error is larger at a model of p
= 3 at close spacing and smaller at large spacing. The opposite occurs for the PCE model
with an order of p = 4. Furthermore, the PCE model with an order of p = 3 seems to have a
low relative error at a downstream distance of S = 3R but this is due to the large amount of
outliers pulling the relative error down. No diﬀerentiation could be made regarding which
model should be used for the power output of the downstream turbine in the ﬁnal model.
The PCE models developed to recreate the response surface of the ﬂapwise DEL showed
no signiﬁcant diﬀerence between the relative error after S > 3R. At S = 3R the error of
the PCE with an order of p = 3 is larger in comparison to the other models, which can be
related due to the DEL within the near wake region. As discussed in the previous section,
the PCE models with an order of p = 5 and p = 6 showed signs of over-ﬁtting. This was
also observed with similar contour plots shown in Appendix J with an order of p = 5 and
p = 6. The PCE model which was created for the DEL of the tower bending moment
around the y-axis showed a larger error range at p = 3 up to a downstream distance of S
= 6R. However, the PCE models with an order of p = [4, 5, 6] did have a larger amount
of outliers. Further downstream the behaviour of the models were similar. This made it
diﬃcult to pinpoint which model to use. Based on this analysis, a cost model was created
with a PCE model of an order of p = 3 for the DEL of the ﬂapwise bending moment and
the tower bending moment around the y-axis. This was done to reduce the complexity of
the model.

6.2
Optimal Layout
This section describes the optimal yaw-conﬁguration for the case of two turbines in a row.
It describes the optimal conﬁguration for a power driven optimization and the optimal
conﬁguration with the fatigue loads included. The aim is to show how the fatigue loads
change with regard to the power output.
79
==PAGE:103==
6.2.1
Final PCE Model
In Section 6.1, the relative error of the PCE model for the power output and the equivalent
loads is shown.
After evaluating the numerical results with the PCE models, an order
of p = 3 was used for the upstream turbine in order to model the DEL of the ﬂapwise
bending moment and the tower bending moment. Equation 3.1 will be used to model the
power output of the upstream turbine. For the power output of the downstream turbine
the PCE model with an order of p = 4 will be used. After performing the power based
optimization, which will be shown in Section 6.2.2, and the validation case, which will
be shown in Section 6.3, the order of p = 4 was selected. However, it should be noted
that the power gain during the validation case is below the relative error and that more
research will be required to further improve the model. An order of p = 3 was used for the
equivalent loads of the downstream turbine. A weighting function was assigned for each
PCE model depending on the objective of the optimization. In addition, the PCE models
for the equivalent loads were inverted since the aim is to reduce the fatigue loads, shown
in Equation 6.1 The optimal point was determined by creating a ﬁne mesh of the input
parameters and by determining the maximum data point using a genetic algorithm.
PCETOT (ψ1, ψ2, S) = n1[PCEPower,1(ψ1) + PCEPower,2(ψ1, ψ2, S)]
+ n2

6.2.2
Power Based Optimization
The optimal yaw conﬁguration for a power based optimization is shown in Figure 6.10. This
was accomplished with the PCE model with an order of p = 3 and p = 4 since no diﬀerence
was found with regard to the relative error. In the aforementioned ﬁgure it can be seen that
an optimal power increase is gained when the upstream turbine is yawed negatively and the
downstream turbine is yawed positively. This was also shown in Section 5.2.1 where the
highest power output was obtained when ψ2 is slightly positive. A negative yaw angle was
to be expected since this resulted in the highest wake deﬂection, shown in Section 4.1.3. A
diﬀerence is noticeable between the PCE model with an order of p = 3 and an order of p
= 4, indicating a larger yaw angle for ψ1 and ψ2 for the latter case at close spacing. The
optimal yaw angles are [ψ1, ψ2] = [-9.9◦, 1.38◦] and [ψ1, ψ2] = [-20.1◦, 4.2◦] at S = 5R for
the PCE with an order of p = 3 and p = 4 respectively. This is due to the fact that the
PCE models have a diﬀerent response and, thus, a diﬀerent optimal point. The contour
plot of the cumulative power output at a downstream distance of S = 5R dependant on ψ1
and ψ2 is shown in Figure 6.12. In the ﬁgure it can be seen that the PCE models have a

6.2.3
Load Based Optimization
The load based optimization was performed with the relation shown in Equation 6.1. This
was done by varying the weight for the upstream turbine and for the downstream turbine
for the DEL and the power output. The weights are ordered as followed: [Power, FlapM
WT1, FlapM WT2, MY WT1, TBBM MY WT2]. The starting weight is for each case the
optimization for the power, after which the weight for the power reduces and the weight for
the speciﬁc load case increases until the optimization is performed only for the load case.
Figure 6.10 illustrates how the yaw angle changes for each load case.
• Case 1: FLapM WTG1, [0, 1, 0, 0, 0]
Figure 6.10 shows the load based optimization with respect to the power and the
ﬂapwise bending moment of the upstream turbine. There it illustrates that the yaw
angle of the upstream and downstream wind turbine reduce in magnitude to reduce
the DEL of the ﬂapwise bending moment. The reduction of ψ2 is due to the reduction
of the weighting for the power. The reduction of ψ1 is due to the reduction the DEL of
the ﬂapwise bending moment of the upstream turbine. This can be seen in Figure 6.1,
where the lowest DEL was obtained at 0◦.
• Case 2: FLapM WTG2, [0, 0, 1, 0, 0]
The trend to reduce the DEL is extremely striking, as it requires an increase of ψ2
up to a distance of S = 12R. At a distance larger than S = 12R ψ2 decreases up
to 0◦. The opposite occurs for ψ1 which reduces in magnitude up to S = 12R with
increasing weights for the DEL. It can be seen that at distances larger than S = 12R
ψ1 increases up to 30◦. This is due to a low region of the DEL, shown in Figure J.2
in Appendix J.
• Case 3: TBBM MY WTG1, [0, 0, 0, 1, 0]
The opposite behaviour compared to the DEL of the ﬂapwise bending moment occurs
for the tower bending moment around the y-axis. The lowest DEL is at ψ1 ≈−30◦,
which can be seen in Figure 6.1. This results in that at the sole based optimization
that the ψ1 reaches −30◦. ψ2 changes due to the power output.
• Case 4: TBBM MY WTG2, [0, 0, 0, 0, 1]
ψ1 reduces in magnitude and becomes slightly positive at the sole load based opti-
mization. ψ2 increases in magnitude and reaches the limit of the domain at 30◦if the
optimization was performed with only the DEL.
• Case 5: DEL of upstream and downstream wind turbine [0, 0.25, 0.25, 0.25, 0.25]
In the ﬁnal case shown in Figure 6.10 the optimization was performed by including
the DEL of the upstream and downstream turbine. There a distinct trend is visible,
a reduction in the DEL for each turbine is obtained by reducing the magnitude of ψ1
and increasing ψ2. The yaw angle of the downstream turbine reaches the limit at 30◦
and propagates forward with an increase in the weights.

Figure 6.11 shows the power output and the equivalent loads during each optimization case.
Here, it can be seen that the power output reduces with increasing weight for the DEL. It
can also be seen that the power output initially reduces at closer distances and propagates
further downstream as the weights increase. This indicates that the beneﬁt to reduce the
power output to reduce the DEL is higher at close spacing than at larger spacing. This is
expected since the power output is lower at closer spacing. As the power output is reduced
the DEL for the ﬂapwise bending moment and the tower bending moment is also decreased.
This indicates that a reduction of the DEL is possible with a reduction of the power output.
84
==PAGE:108==
Similar results were obtained in Van Dijk et al. (2016) [84], where a load based optimiza-
tion was performed for a two turbine row conﬁguration with a distance of S = 12R. The
study was conducted by incorporating the FLORIS model and CCblade, which predicts the
power production and the loading of the wind turbine blades based on the blade element
momentum method. The optimization was performed with the NREL5MW turbine. There
it was also found that for the sole load based optimization, an increase of the yaw angle of
the downstream turbine was necessary and a reduction of the upstream yaw angle. They
also concluded that load reduction is possible at a cost of power. However, it should be
noted that the weighting of the DEL load during the study is not known.
6.3
Model Validation
This section describes the validation of the created PCE model to optimize the cumulative
power output and the fatigue loading. Figure 6.12 highlights the normalized contours of
the power output of the upstream turbine and the downstream turbine with respect to ψ1
and ψ2 at a downstream distance of S = 5R. It can be seen that a positive yaw angle for
the downstream turbine results into a higher power output if the upstream turbine is yawed
with a negative yaw angle. The validation was done by performing a blind test with the
numerical simulation in Ellipsys3D with a spacing of S = 5R with ψ1 = -15◦and ψ2 = 5◦.
At this point both PCE models showed a region of increase of the power output, illustrated
in Figure 6.12. Furthermore, the numerical simulation in Ellipsys3D was done with seven
turbines as the spacing was reduced in order to investigate the eﬀect within a wind farm
shown in Figure 6.13. This will be discussed in the next section. Table 14 tabulates the
normalized power of the downstream turbine with respect to the upstream turbine at ψ1
= 0◦and at ψ2 = 0◦with the power output of the "ghost" turbine. It shows that the
estimation of the cumulative power gain of the downstream turbine by the PCE model was
over predicting for an order of p = 3 and p = 4. A lower power gain was achieved with
the simulations of the "ghost" turbines. The output of the PCE with an order of p = 4
shows a better comparison to the output of the "ghost" turbine. However, it should be
noted that the relative error is larger than the predicted power gain. This indicates that
more simulation cases are required to validate and improve the PCE model. In addition,
a higher power output was achieved for the "LES" turbine for the upstream turbine and
the downstream turbine. This could be related to the induction model used with the AL
model and the standalone FLEX5 model.

6.4
Summary
This chapter explained the performed steps to develop a model, based on the polynomial
chaos expansion, that predicts the eﬀect of the equivalent loads and the power output with
respect to the yaw oﬀset.
Individual models are created for the power output and the
equivalent loads of the upstream and downstream turbine. At ﬁrst, the response of the
diﬀerent models were evaluated with the measurement data and with each other. This was
done by determining if a model was over ﬁtting and by evaluating the relative error.
After evaluating the diﬀerent models, A new model was created that combines the individual
PCE models for the power output and the equivalent loads. A power based optimization
and a load based optimization was then performed with the ﬁnal model. This analysis
showed that a high power gain due to yaw steering was achieved at close spacing, requiring
a large negative yaw angle for the upstream turbine and a slight positive yaw angle for
the downstream turbine. This is related to the increased power output when the deﬂected
wake approaches the rotor perpendicular. At larger spacing the power gain was reduced
and, with it, the magnitude of the yaw angle for the upstream and downstream turbine.
This indicates that steering the wake is more beneﬁcial at a small spacing. Furthermore,
during the optimization process of the yaw conﬁguration it was identiﬁed that a reduction
of the DEL could be obtained at the cost of power production. However, it should be noted
that the relative error of the model is higher than the power gain, this indicates that the
model needs to be further improved. This can be done by simulating at speciﬁc yaw angles
where the response surface has an unexpected behaviour. Furthermore, the model proved
to provide better results within the near wake region in comparison to the FLORIS model.
However, the developed model is only valid for this case study. The last section gives a
description of the validation of the developed PCE model.
88
==PAGE:112==
7
Conclusion and Future Works
Conclusion
Measurement data, numerical results and engineering tools were used to investigate the
wake characteristics. The numerical results are obtained with the ﬂowsolver Ellipsys3D &
LES and the FLORIS model was used to analytically determine the wake characteristics.
The measurement data are obtained from a full scale ﬁeld test conducted at the SWiFT
facility. Through the use of the numerical simulation and the aerolastic tool Flex5 the
response of the wind turbine at multiple downstream distances was determined. With the
obtained data from the aerolastic tool Flex5 a model was then created which combines the
fatigue loads and the power output. The aim of this study is to provide a fundamental un-
derstanding of applying a yaw-based wind farm control and to highlight the main challenges
by investigating the wake characteristics, the turbine response and the implementation of
a yaw based wind farm control.
The analysis of the full scale ﬁeld test shows that the wake experiences a deﬂection due to a
yaw angle of the upstream wind turbine. However, this was performed for a time domain of
3 hours with a turbine that changed its yaw angle continuously ever 2-3 min. This reduces
the amount of data to determine the relation between the wake center and the yaw angle.
Furthermore, a correction was necessary for a yaw misalignment of the upstream turbine.
This is followed by the comparison with the numerical results, which showed a discrep-
ancy between the velocity deﬁcit. This can be related to the diﬀerence of the atmospheric
stability in the numerical simulations and the ﬁeld test, the diﬀerence in the turbulence
parameters at diﬀerent heights and the use of a single turbulence seed. Simulations with
multiple turbulence seeds and a longer time domain will increase the statistical conﬁdence.
During the comparison between the numerical simulation and the FLORIS model it was
observed that the induction zone was not modelled. In addition, it was observed that the
near wake region was modelled using the assumption of the potential core resulting in a
large velocity deﬁcit. Furthermore, the FLORIS model is not capable to predict the asym-
metry of the wake based on the turbine model and the inﬂow conditions. In the FLORIS
model the assumption was used to model the wake as a 2D Gaussian. This indicates that
it is not able to predict the shape of the wake accurately as the wake follows a kidney
shape in the numerical simulation. In the FLORIS model the shape of the wake remains
symmetric which inﬂuences the power output of the downstream turbine. However, the
aim of the FLORIS model is to predict the velocity deﬁcit and not the wake shape. During
the analysis of the wake characteristics, a smaller near wake region was observed at large
yaw angles, which indicates that the wake breaks down faster. This could be due to the
introduced instabilities on the turbine wake when the rotor is yawed. Furthermore, it was
observed that the wake of the downstream turbine is deﬂected when the upstream turbine
has a large yaw angle, which is known as "secondary steering". Finally, a low correlation
89
==PAGE:113==
was found between the instantaneous wake center and the thrust. This highlights the dif-
ﬁculty to steer the wake instantaneously.
The analysis of the turbine response of the upstream and downstream turbine focused on
the relation between the yaw angle and the power output and the DEL of the wind turbine
components. The DEL due to the ﬂapwise bending moment, the edgewise bending mo-
ment and the tower bending moment around each axis was investigated. The power of the
upstream turbine reduced as the yaw angle increased. The DEL of the upstream turbine
varied with the yaw angle. It showed that the DEL, due to the ﬂapwise bending moment,
increased for large yaw angles. Due to the tower bending moment around the x-axis and
the y-axis, the DEL was reduced. The DEL of the edgewise bending moment and the tower
bending moment around the z-axis showed a reduction of the DEL at negative yaw angles
and an increase at positive yaw angles. The power output of the downstream turbine is
higher when the upstream turbine has a negative yaw angle in comparison to a positive
yaw angle. This is due to the larger wake deﬂection at a negative yaw angle. After analyz-
ing the power output of the downstream turbine, the equivalent loads of the downstream
turbine was investigated with regard to the yaw angle of the upstream and the downstream
wind turbine. There it showed that the DEL is dependent on the velocity deﬁcit and the
turbulence structures within the wake. Due to the deﬂection of the wake, the calculated
DEL was altered. This could result in an increase or a decrease of the DEL. In addition,
the eﬀect of partial wake loading was noticeable. During the analysis of four turbines in a
row, it was observed that a power gain was achieved for the second, third and fourth wind
turbine. This indicates that the power gain is not only beneﬁcial for the second turbine.
With the obtained data from the aerolastic tool Flex5 a model was created which combines
the fatigue loads and the power output. The model is build using the polynomial chaos
expansion theory and showed that a high power gain due to yaw steering was achieved at
close spacing, requiring a large negative yaw angle for the upstream turbine and a slight
positive yaw angle for the downstream turbine.
This is related to the increased power
output when the deﬂected wake approaches the rotor perpendicular. At larger spacing the
power gain was reduced and, with it, the magnitude of the yaw angle for the upstream and
downstream turbine. This indicates that steering the wake is more beneﬁcial at a small
spacing.
Furthermore, during the optimization process of the yaw conﬁguration it was
identiﬁed that a reduction of the DEL could be obtained at the cost of power production.
However, it should be noted that the relative error of the model is higher than the power
gain, this indicates that the model needs to be further improved. This can be done by
simulating at speciﬁc yaw angles where the response surface has an unexpected behaviour.
Furthermore, the model proved to provide better results within the near wake region in
comparison to the FLORIS model. However, the developed model is only valid for this case
study.
90
==PAGE:114==
Future Works
• Full scale ﬁeld measurements: The data obtained from the full scale ﬁeld test was
limited to a time domain of 3 hours with a turbine that changed its yaw angle contin-
uously every 2-3 min. This reduced the amount of data points which could be used to
analyze the eﬀect of the wake center with regard to the yaw angle. More measurement
data will be required to improve the statistical conﬁdence. This also aids the process
to detect the dependency of the wake center related to the wind speed, the turbulence
intensity, the atmospheric stability and the shear proﬁle. In addition, the eﬀect of
yawing the turbine with a larger delay between each change in the yaw heading needs
to be investigated. Furthermore, obtaining load measurements will help to determine
the behaviour of the wind turbine within the ﬁeld. It will also aid the process to
validate the performance of the aerolastic tool Flex5 during yawed operation.
• Wake Dynamics: The eﬀect of the wake at diﬀerent conditions could not be analyzed,
as the dynamics of the wake were investigated at one inﬂow condition. Furthermore,
more simulations with diﬀerent turbulence seeds are required to increase the statistical
conﬁdence of the results. This also gives a better understanding of the inﬂuence of the
yaw angles on the meandering phenomena of the wake. Furthermore, the correlation
of the thrust with regard to the wake center needs to be further investigated. This
is of major interest since it determines whether yawing the wake instantaneously is
possible. More research will also be required to determine the eﬀect of the induction
zone of the downstream turbine during yaw based operation.
• FLORIS model calibration: It was observed that a better performance of the FLORIS
model was achieved when the parameter α was tuned accordingly. These were deter-
mined in Bastankhah et al. (2016) [9] which also stated that the available data was
not suﬃcient to provide a reliable and constant coeﬃcient. Furthermore, it was shown
in Appendix D that the skew angle diﬀered depending on the yaw angle. Including
this correction in the FLORIS model demonstrated that the wake had an asymmetric
behaviour similar. More research will be necessary to determine if the change in the
skew angle is observed with diﬀerent turbulence seeds, other inﬂow conditions and
turbulence models. This might have implications for the calculations of the loading
on the wind turbine since it uses the same relation for the skew angle in the yaw
model.
• Turbine Response: The response of the wind turbine was investigated at one inﬂow
condition with α = 0.18 and TI = 0.143 [-]. In Fleming et al. (2014) [29] and in Ennis
et al. (2018) [21] diﬀerent results were obtained. In the former study, the NREL5MW
turbine was used with a shear exponent of 0.11[-], a TI ≈0.06 [-] and a wind speed
of 8 [m/s]. In the later study, the V27 turbine was used but with a shear exponent of
around 0.4[-], a TI ≈0.07 [-] and a wind speed of 7 [m/s]. Besides the diﬀerence in the
91
==PAGE:115==
inﬂow conditions the turbine model was diﬀerent, the V27 turbine is stiﬀer than the
NREL5MW wind turbine. This shows that more research is necessary to determine
the relation of a yawed wind turbine with regard to the TI, the shear exponent, the
wind speed and the wind turbine model with regard to the DEL.
• PCE model: The developed PCE model is limited to the case study at one wind
speed and one shear exponent. More research will be necessary to expand the model
and increase the amount of input variables, such as the wind speed, the turbulence
intensity, the shear proﬁle, the atmospheric stability and the turbine model. However,
this will signiﬁcantly increase the complexity of the model. In order to reduce the
complexity, the model can be limited to the region of interest at conditions in which
yaw steering has the largest eﬀect. This requires an investigation of the inﬂuence of
each parameter separately.
92
==PAGE:116==
Appendix A
Wake Center Detection
This appendix gives a short overview of the method used to determine the wake center,
which was used during the analysis in Chapter 4. As described in the Section 3, a DTU
SpinnerLidar was mounted on the nacelle of the WTGa1 Turbine directed to the rear of the
turbine measuring at a downstream distance of S ≈5R. It measures each scan in a Rosetta
pattern every 2 s with 984 data points. As mentioned in Section 3, the yaw heading was
changing continuously. This made it necessary to determine the wake center within the
spatial domain in order to investigate the wake behaviour at diﬀerent yaw angles.
A.1
Velocity Deﬁcit
The velocity deﬁcit was determined by extracting the mean wind proﬁle from each Lidar
scan. This was done by ﬁrst interpolating the measurement data with a Rosetta pattern
onto a 1x1 m grid. Since the sonic anemometers are located at 5 diﬀerent heights, a wind
proﬁle can be generated for the entire time period of the measurement campaign. The
mean wind proﬁle is created by performing a linear interpolation between each height onto
the grid. The velocity deﬁcit was calculated by subtracting the horizontal wind speed from
the horizontal component of the mean wind proﬁle. Here, the line of sight velocity of the
Lidar was projected onto the horizontal plane using the approach described in Machefaux
et al.(2012) [50]. This method is based on the assumption that the vertical wind speed and
lateral wind speed are much smaller than the stream-wise wind speed. Since the SWiFT
facility is located on a ﬂat terrain and the w-component was negligible the assumption was
applicable. Furthemore, a correction is necessary to account for the yaw angle and the tilt
angle of the Lidar (4 [◦]) with respect to the ground. Here, θpan and θtilt are the pan angle
and the tilt angle respectively.
Uhorizontal = ULos · (cos(θpan + θyaw) · cos(θtilt,Lidar + θtilt,Turbine))
(A.1)
A.2
Gaussian Method & Center of Mass Method
After determining the velocity deﬁcit for each scan, the wake center was determined for
each scan. This was done by applying two diﬀerent methods. The ﬁrst is based on the
Gaussian Fit and the second is based on the Center of Mass. This was done to assure
the determination of the wake center with two diﬀerent methods and validate the results
with both methods through comparison. The characteristics of the wake was described in
section 2.1.1. Keane et al. (2016) [45] stated that the shape of the wake can be modeled
with a double peak Gaussian distribution within the near wake and a single peak Gaussian
distribution in the far wake. The transition between the near wake region and the far wake
region depends on the speciﬁcation of the wind turbine and the atmospheric conditions.
93
==PAGE:117==
Since it was not known in which region the Lidar was measuring a method had to be
developed that determines the wake center in both regions. This was done by extracting
the normalized horizontal velocity deﬁcit proﬁle at hub height and ﬁt an one peak Gaussian
distribution and a double peak Gaussian distribution. Depending on the goodness of the
ﬁt, a single or double peak Gaussian distribution is used to determine the center of the
wake. The coeﬃcient of determination R2 was used to perform the selection between the
two Gaussian distributions. If R2 is below 0.6 for the single peak Gaussian, a double peak
Gaussian is used. This value was chosen arbitrarily and, therefore, needs to be further
analyzed. Due to the stochastic nature of the wind, the Gaussian distribution will always
have a higher R2. This is to ensure that the double peak Gaussian distribution will be
applied if two distinct peaks can be identiﬁed. In the case that R2 also resulted in a value
lower than 0.6 for the double Gaussian distribution the tool resulted in no wake center for
the given scan. After determining the wake center with the extracted horizontal velocity
proﬁle, the vertical velocity proﬁle was extracted at the horizontal location of the wake
center. The same method was then used to determine the vertical position of the wake.
Figure A.1 gives a schematics of the steps involved to determine the wake center.
fgauss,1D = ae
(x−µ)2
2σ2
(A.2)
fgauss,2D = a1e
(x−µ1)2
2σ2
1
+ a2e
(x−µ2)2
2σ2
2
(A.3)
Figure A.1: Flow diagram to determine the wake center based on the Gaussian distribution
The second method to determine the wake center was done by calculating the center of mass
of the velocity deﬁcit below a certain threshold. This threshold was set to be µV,def −1.5σ
94
==PAGE:118==
according to Taylor & Johnson
[76] where σ is the temporal standard deviation of the
wind. The advantage of the Center of Mass method is that it quickly determines the wake
center regardless of the wake shape.
After determining the wake center within the Lidar scan, the location needs to be trans-
formed to the SWiFT coordinate system. This is illustrated in Figure A.3. In addition, the
equation used to transform the coordinate system to the reference plane of the turbine is
shown in Equation A.4.
yWake,Turb = yWake,Lidar · cos(ψ) + sin(ψ) · xwake,Lidar
(A.4)
A.3
Evaluation
Figure A.2 gives an example of the wake center detection. Here, it can be seen that the
detection of the wake center is extremely sensitive with the center of mass method with
regard to the Gaussian method, due to multiple low wind speed regions in a single Lidar
scan. The center detected with the Gaussian ﬁt showed to be less sensitive and will be used
during the analysis in Chapter 4. In addition, it is widely used as the industry standard
and was used in Fleming et. al. (2014) [29] which enables a direct comparison.

center of the Lidar scan on the 16th of December

Appendix B
Wake Boundary Detection
This appendix explains the steps performed to determine the wake boundary of each in-
dividual Lidar scan. It is based on the Gaussian distribution in order to provide a direct
comparison with the results of Fleming et. al. [30].
The boundary of the wake was determined by ﬁtting a 2D-Gaussian distribution on a radial
grid, which is based on the same principal applied to the wake center detection described
in Appendix A. The ﬂow diagram in Figure B.5 shows the steps performed to determine
the wake boundary. The ﬁrst step is to determine the wake center, from which the radial
grid is built. In order to reduce the computational time and ease the ﬁtting procedure of
the Gaussian distribution, a maximum distance of 2.5R between the most outer point of
the radial grid and the center was set. A Gaussian distribution was then ﬁtted along one
radial line at every azimuth angle. Depending on the Gaussian distribution, the boundary
was set to be equal to (µ+σ, µ−σ) or (µ1 −σ1, µ2 + σ2) for a single Gaussian, or a double
Gaussian distribution respectively, shown in Figure B.3 and in Figure B.4. The goodness
of the ﬁtted for the Gaussian distribution depended on several criteria. One of which was
R2, which had to have a value higher than 0.6. Other criteria were µ which needed to be
along the radial grid points and σ which could not be larger than 2.5R. In the case that the
R2 is below 0.6 no boundary points were determined at the speciﬁc azimuth angle. After
determining the location of the boundary at each azimuth angle, a spline was ﬁt with the
radial distance and the azimuth angle to ﬁll the missing gaps. Subsequently, the spline was
interpolated onto the Cartesian grid of the moving reference frame.

Appendix D
FLORIS Corrections
During the analysis of the FLORIS model, two possible corrections were determined that
improve the outcome of the FLORIS model. The ﬁrst correction is related to the parameter
α, which will be discussed ﬁrst. This is followed by the second correction related to the
skew angle χ.
D.1
α-Parameter
As described in Section 4.1.2, the FLORIS model uses the potential core concept to calcu-
late the wake characteristics of the near wake region. Equation 2.17 in Section 2.3 is used
to determine the length of the near wake region, which depends on the parameters α and β.
These were determined in Bastankhah et al. (2016) [9] which also stated that the available
data was not suﬃcient to provide a reliable and constant coeﬃcient.
The parameter α was determined with Equation 2.17, which needed as an input the near
wake length and β = 0.077.
The results for α are shown in Figure D.1, ranging from
α ≈[1.1, 1.24]. This is an increase of a factor of 2 in comparison to the original data.
The comparison of the velocity component
u
u∞between the numerical simulation and the
FLORIS model is shown in Figure D.6. There it can be seen that the wake deﬂection with
α = 1.19 has a closer resemblance to the numerical simulation both were determined with
the Gaussian method described in Appendix A. Furthermore, the horizontal normalized
velocity deﬁcit proﬁle at various downstream distances are illustrated in Figure D.2 for the
parameter α = 0.58[−] and α = 1.19[−] at ψ1 = 0[◦] and ψ1 = −30[◦], highlighting that
α = 1.19[−] shows a better agreement with the numerical simulations. This implies, as
stated in Bastankhah et al., (2016) [9] that the derived parameter α is not reliable and a
constant coeﬃcient.

D.2
Skew Angle
The deﬂection angle in the FLORIS model is calculated using Equation 2.18 in Section 2.3.
It depends on the skew angle and was derived using Equation 2.20. The latter equation
was derived using the relation derived by Burton et al. (1995) [13] shown in Equation E.7
in Appendix E.2, which is based on the vortex theory. It is based on the assumption that
the variation of the circulation around a mean value has a small eﬀect on the induced
velocity and that the wake is dominated by the vorticity shed from the blade tips by the
mean value of the circulation [13]. An illustration is shown in Figure D.3. Through this
assumption Equation D.1 was derived, from which the relation in Equation E.7 was deter-
mined. This states that the skew angle is symmetric for a positive and a negative yaw angle.
However, the wake has an asymmetric behaviour with regard to the deﬂection which was
described in Section 4.1.3 and in Fleming et al. (2018) [24]. A possible explanation for this
phenomena (given in Section 4.1.3) is that the strength of the vorticity is inﬂuenced by the
shear proﬁle. Since the deﬂection of the wake is asymmetric, it implies that the relation
shown in Equation E.7 for the skew angle diﬀers depending on a positive or negative yaw
angle in the case a shear proﬁle is present.
100
==PAGE:124==
Figure D.3: The deﬂected vortex wake of a yawed rotor
showing the shed vortices of three blades [13]
tanχ = U∞(sinψ −atanχ
2 )
U∞(cosψ −a)
(D.1)
As the transition point between the near wake and the far wake was determined in Sec-
tion 4.1.5 and the location of the wake center was known at a certain yaw angle, it was
possible to determine the skew angle from the numerical simulations. This was done with
the simple geometric equation χ = asin( δ
x0 ) + ψ. The results are shown in Figure D.4,
including the relation from Equation E.7. It can be noticed that the slope diﬀers depend-
ing if the rotor is yawed positively or negatively.
The slope of the relation is changed
accordingly to match the slope determined of the numerical simulations, which resulted
in χ = (1 + 0.3a)ψ for positive yaw angles and χ = (1 + 0.5a)ψ for negative yaw angles.
The former equation was implemented in the FLORIS model for ψ1 = 30[◦] and the result
is shown in Figure D.5. The deﬂection obtained from the FLORIS model resembles the
deﬂection obtained from the numerical simulation. This implies that the current yaw model
might need to be adapted to account for the shear proﬁle. This also implies that the aero-
lastic codes, such as Flex5 described in Appendix E, might provide incorrect results when
the turbine is yawed with a shear proﬁle. These corrections to the FLORIS model were not
enforced during the analysis in Chapter 5 since it is outside the scope of this thesis. The
aim is to highlight the shortcomings of the current FLORIS model. In addition, the goal
is to provide a thorough understanding of the used assumptions in order to determine the
loads during yaw conﬁgurations, which will be shown in Chapter 5.
