Appendix E
Unsteady BEM
This appendix describes the unsteady BEM code, which is widely used in areolastic tools
such as Flex5. The aim is to provide a better understanding of the unsteady BEM method
and highlight the main diﬀerences in comparison to the steady BEM code.
It will not
show the exact steps to create the unsteady BEM code. In addition, an overview of the
used assumptions and models used to determine the response of the turbine during yaw-
misalignment in current aerolastic tools is given. Focus will be given to the yaw model,
which is used in the Flex5 tool. The exact steps to create the unsteady BEM model is
given in Branlard et. al.(2017) [12] and in Hansen (2015) [38]. A simpliﬁed unsteady BEM
code was created and was used during the analysis in Chapter 5.1 to understand the local
response of each individual blade of the turbine. The well known steady BEM method is
suﬃcient to estimate the annual energy production at a certain site. However, in order to
simulate realistic aerolastic behaviour of the wind turbine, the unsteadiness of the wind
needs to be incorporated, such as the wind shear and the atmospheric turbulence. One
possible option is using the unsteady BEM code.
E.1
Architecture of the Unsteady BEM method
Due to unsteadiness of the wind seen by the rotor, an unsteady BEM method is a possible
option to simulate the aerolastic behaviour of the wind turbine. It includes the variations
in the incoming wind, unsteady ﬂow disturbances due to yaw & tilt and motions of the
structure. Since the wind changes in time and space, it is important to know the exact
location of each blade segment and each time step.
This was done by implementing a
transformation matrix at each segment. Figure 3.12 in Section 3.4 gives an example of a
simple model of a wind turbine, indicating the reference frame at the turbine from which
the transformation matrix was determined. With the use of the transformation matrix, the
relative velocity component was determined at each segment, shown in Equation E.1 and
in Equation E.2. There the relative velocity is dependant on the induced velocity (W) and
the rotational speed of the rotor (ω). The parameter dr indicates the distance from the
rotor center to the center of the segment.
Vrel = V0 + W
(E.1)
Vrel,y
Vrel,z


Vy
Vz




Wy
Wz

(E.2)
The induced velocity is calculated using Equation E.3. This can be further elaborated using
Figure E.1. Due to the thrust, a velocity normal to the rotor plane is induced deﬂecting
the wake, as described in Section 2.1.3. Within the wake, the induced velocity is twice
as the velocity induced in the rotor plane, based on simple momentum theory shown in
Hansen (2015) [38]. The relation between the thrust and the induced velocity is shown
103
==PAGE:127==
in EquationE.3 and was stated by Bramwell (1976) [11] based on a gyrocopter in forward
ﬂight. At zero yaw, this equation reduces to the steady BEM method. Furthermore, the
assumption is made that the lift is the only component inducing the velocity and that the
induction velocity acts in the opposite direction of the lift. The induction velocity at each
blade segment is determined using Equation E.4 for the normal induced velocity. Equa-
tion E.5 is used to determine the tangential induced velocity. Here, F is Prandtl’s tip loss
factor, B is the number of blades, FL is the lift, ρ is the density and φ is angle between the
rotor plane and the relative velocity.
Figure E.1: Wake behind a rotor disc [38].
Wn = n · W =

2ρA|V′|
(E.3)
Wn = Wz =
−B · FL · cos(φ)
4πρrF|V0 + fgn(n · W)| (E.4)
Wt = Wy =
−B · FL · sin(φ)
4πρrF|V0 + fgn(n · W)|
(E.5)
The induced velocity needs to be determined at each timestep. This requires the blades to
rotate, which is not the case with the steady BEM method. At each timestep the induced
velocity is determined at each blade segment, from which the relative velocity and angle
of attack was determined. Through the use of tabulated data, the thrust and the power
coeﬃcient can be computed. This will lead to the local blade loading at each blade segment.
In the case of aerolastic computations the vibration of the structure needs to be included.
During the analysis, the turbine was assumed stiﬀand the aerolastic simulations in Flex5
were performed with a stiﬀturbine.
E.2
Yaw Model
The yaw model was implemented by using Equation E.6 and equation E.7 in the unsteady
BEM code. A yaw model is necessary since there will be an azimuthal variation of the
induced velocity and the thrust. A smaller induced velocity and a higher thrust is present
when the blade is located "upwind" and a larger induced velocity and a lower thrust is
present when the blade is located "downwind", when viewing it form above. The yaw model
presented in Equation E.6 is proposed by Glauert (1926) [36]. The angle between the wind
velocity and the rotational axis is know as the skew angle (χ). Furthermore, W0 is the mean
induced velocity at an annular ring determined with Equation E.4 and Equation E.5. θw is
104
==PAGE:128==
the azimuth angle where the blade is the deepest into the wake. The skew angle is assumed
to be constant along the entire blade and is determined using the mean induced velocity at
a radial position close to r/R ≈0.7. The yaw model is implemented by ﬁrst determining
the induced velocity at each blade segment and then apply the correction within one time
step.
W = W0(1 + r
Rtan(X
2 )cos(θwing −θw)
(E.6)
X = (0.6Wr/R≈0.7 + 1)ψ
(E.7)
E.3
Validating the unsteady BEM method
The unsteady BEM model is validated by comparing it to the results obtained from the
aerolastic tool Flex5. In order to have a direct comparison a stiﬀturbine was simulated
without the aerodynamic eﬀects of the tower. The simpliﬁed unsteady BEM code and the
aerolastic tool Flex5 was simulated with uniform inﬂow condition with V = 8.328 m/s. A
diﬀerence is noticeable with the outcome of the simpliﬁed unsteady BEM code and the
simulation results of Flex5 as the unsteady BEM was not equipped with the dynamic stall
model and the dynamic wake model. Furthermore, since the source code for Flex5 was not
available, the corrections for the tip-loss, hub-loss and the correction due to the momentum
theory breakdown was not known. However, a similar behaviour of the thrust is detected
for a positive and negative yaw angle compared to Flex5. The performance of the simpliﬁed
unsteady BEM code was deemed to be suﬃcient since it was able to predict the behaviour
of the thrust at diﬀerent yaw angles.
Figure E.2: Validating the unsteady BEM code with Flex5

Appendix F
Partial Wake
This appendix will describe the behaviour of the turbine by shifting it along a horizontal
plane, shown in Figure F.1 and with various yaw angles (ψ2). This analysis was conducted
to compare the behaviour of the turbine with the behaviour of the turbine when the wake
is deﬂected. Furthermore, the aim is to achieve a better understanding of the partial wake
on the DEL of the downstream turbine and the power output. This was done by extracting
the ﬂowﬁeld at a downstream distance of S = 10R and move it across the rotor plane in
the aerolastic tool FLEX5. Here, a positive shift indicates a shift upwards when viewed
from above. The downstream turbine was shifted with steps of 0.25 in order to reduce the
computational time.
Figure F.1: Lay-out consisting of 2 turbines with an incoming wind, the downstream turbine is swept through the
wake of the upstream turbine
F.1
Power Output
The normalized power output is shown in Figure F.2 for the downstream turbine shifted
across a horizontal plane at S = 10R. Here, the power was normalized with the downstream
turbine at S = 10R with ψ2 = 0◦and δy
R = 0[−]. A similar trend was observed in Van
Dijk et al. (2016) [84], where an increase in power is obtained by shifting it positive or
negative. This is expected since the rotor will be able to extract more energy at partial wake
compared to full wake as the power is an integral quantity. As discussed in Section 5.1 the
turbine experiences a power loss at large yaw angle, which results into the saddle function
in Figure F.2. In addition, the power output is not fully symmetric over the range of δy
R .
This can be related due to the rotational direction of the rotor blade on the wak described
in Van Dijk et al.
(2016) [84].
The comparison between the normalized power output
of the downstream turbine obtained by shifting the turbine across a horizontal plane and
by deﬂecting the wake is shown in Figure F.3.
The comparison was done by selecting
the power of the shifted turbine closest to the lateral position of the deﬂected wake at
ψ1 = ±30 & ψ1 = 0◦at S = 10R. The power of the downstream turbine with no lateral
shift and no deﬂection match, which is to be expected. Two details can be seen during the
comparison: the higher power output and the maximum output is obtained at ≈±5◦. The
latter detail is due to the deﬂected wake. A higher power output is obtained when the wake

is perpendicular to the rotor shown in Section 4. The higher power output is a combination
of faster breakdown of the wake and that the wake is following a kidney shape at very
large yaw angles at a downstream distance. The wake breaks down faster since the thrust
component is reduced, leading to a lower velocity deﬁcit within the wake. Furthermore,
at large yaw angles, the wake follows a kidney shape, discussed in Section 2.1. Due to
the kidney shape, the wake "wraps" around the turbine through which higher velocities
will reach the rotor area. These eﬀects result into a higher power output in comparison
to shifting the downstream turbine. However, it should be noted that the position of the
lateral shift and the deﬂected wake are not exactly the same.
F.2
Fatigue Loads
The normalized fatigue loads for the ﬂapwise bending moment and the edgewise bending
moment is shown in Figure F.4. The fatigue loads were normalized with the same turbine
conﬁguration as discussed in the previous section. At ψ2 = 0◦the DEL of the ﬂapwise
bending moment remains almost similar with a positive and a negative shift. An increase
of the DEL is noticed at larger yaw angles. The DEL of the edgewise bending moment
increase with ψ1 > 30◦and decreases with ψ2 < 0◦. This due to the azimuthal variation of
the thrust and the rotation of the wind turbine.

Appendix G
Aﬀect of the Cone Angle on the Turbine Re-
sponse
This appendix describes the eﬀect of the cone angle on the power output and the fatigue
loads. This was done since most modern turbines have a cone angle in order to provide
clearance between the blade and the tower. Introducing a cone angle also eﬀects the fatigue
loads, since it inﬂuences the velocity triangle on each blade segment. Figure G.1 gives an
illustration of how the deﬂected ﬂow interacts with a coned rotor reorienting the velocity
within the wake. The ﬂowﬁelds from the numerical simulations performed with Ellipsys3D
described in Section 2.2 were again used as an input to the aerolastic tool Flex5. The only
diﬀerence is the cone angle of the V27 model at -5◦, allowing for a direct comparison of the
fatigue loads of the downstream turbine. In the Section 5.2 the DEL was determined with
a cone angle of 0◦.
(a) No Cone Angle
(b) With Cone Angle
Figure G.1: Illustration of the interaction between the deﬂected ﬂow and the cone angle
G.1
Power Output
The power output is visualized in Figure G.2. As expected, the power behaves similar to
the power output with no cone angle, shown in Section 5.2. This is since the power is
an integrated quantity and is determined by the velocity ﬁeld over the entire rotor plane.
However since the total rotor area decreases slightly the power reduces slightly in comparison
to the turbine with no cone angle.

(f) FlapM, ψ1 = 30◦
Figure G.3: DEL loads of the ﬂapwise bending moment were determined with Flex5 normalized with the DEL of
the upstream turbine at S = 0R with ψ1 = 0◦. a, b, c: DEL at ψ1 = [−30◦, 0◦, 30◦] with diﬀerent spacing and yaw
angles for the downstream turbine. d, e, f:
Diﬀerence between the DEL of the upstream and downstream turbine.
Figure G.3 shows the DEL of the ﬂapwise bending moment. The ﬁrst noticeable detail is
that the normalized DEL is higher compared to the normalized DEL with no cone angle.
This is visible with the DEL of the downstream turbine and the diﬀerence of the DEL
between the upstream and downstream turbine. Furthermore, it can be seen that the DEL
is almost similar at ψ1 = 0◦at ψ < 0◦and at ψ > 0◦. The small diﬀerence can be due to
the slight shift of the wake at 0◦shown in Section 4.1.3. However, it can be seen that at
ψ1 = 30◦the diﬀerence between the DEL at ψ2 > 0◦and at ψ2 < 0◦is larger than with
ψ1 = −30◦. This could be due to the orientation of the wake onto the rotor. Creating a
larger diﬀerence between the azimuthal loading when yawed negative compared to a positive
yaw. The same eﬀect was noticed with no cone angle but the diﬀerence was less large. The
DEL is increased at ψ1 = −30◦and ψ2 > 30◦. This was also noticed in the case with
111
==PAGE:135==
no cone angle and which could be due to the orientation of the wake onto the rotor. At
ψ1 = −30◦the same behaviour is observed as with no cone angle. A large DEL at close
distance which reduces further downstream at ψ2 = 0◦that the DEL increases at large yaw
angles. Analyzing the diﬀerence between the upstream and downstream turbine shows that
the DEL has the same trend as with no cone angle.

Appendix H
Comparison Ellipsys3D and SOWFA
This appendix shows the comparison between the results obtained by Fleming et al. (2014) [29]
with the NREL5MW turbine and the simulations performed with the V27 turbine. The
simulations performed by Fleming et al.(2014) has a turbulence intensity of 6.3%, a shear
exponent of 0.11. In comparison to a turbulence intensity of 14.3% and a shear exponent
of 0.18 in Ellipsys3D.

Appendix I
Code to build the PCE model
I.1
Scaling the input parameters
yaw_1_scaled = Input [0]/30
yaw_2_scaled = Input [1]/30
Distance_scaled = Input [2]/ np.max(Input [2])
Power_scaled = Input [3]/ np.max(Input [3])
Dist_max = np.max(Input [2])
P_max = np.max(Input [3])
Listing 1: Scaling the input parameters
I.2
K-Fold of the PC-model
Input = [yaw_1_scaled , yaw_2_scaled , Distance_scaled , Power_scaled]
#The
polynomial
basis is created
based on the given
input
distribution of the input
variables
#In this case the Hermite
polynomial
and the
Legendre
polynomial
will be used to create the
multivariate
polynomial
distribution = cp.J(cp.Normal (0, 4.95/30) , cp.Normal (0, 4.95/30) , cp
.Uniform (0, 1))
orthogonal_expansion_ttr = cp.orth_ttr (3, distribution )
rs_p3 = []
Matrix = []
for drand in range(0, 9):
#90% of the input
conditions
are used to create the PC model
#the
selection is performed
randomly
I_rand = random.sample(range (1, len(Input [0])), int (0.9* len(
Input [0])))
#The
coefficients
are
determined
using the
regression
method
approx_model_ttr_p3
= cp. fit_regression (
orthogonal_expansion_ttr , [Input [0][ I_rand],Input [1][ I_rand],
Input [2][ I_rand ]], Input [3][ I_rand ])
P_all = approx_model_ttr_p3 (Input [0], Input [1], Input [2])
#Selecting
the
remaining
10% of the input
variables
I_inv = np.zeros(len(Input [0]))
I_inv[I_rand] = 1
I_inv = (I_inv
==0)
117
==PAGE:141==
#RMSE is calculated
using the
remaining
10%
rs_p3.append(np.sqrt(sum ((( Input [3][ I_inv] - P_all[I_inv ])/P_all
[I_inv ]) **2)/len(P_all[I_inv ])))
Coefs = []
for dq in range(0, len( approx_model_ttr_p3 .keys)):
a = approx_model_ttr_p3 .A[ approx_model_ttr_p3 .keys[dq]]
Coefs.append(a.tolist ())
Matrix.append(Coefs)
#The
coefficients in the final PC model
consists of the
average of
the
previous PC models.
RMSE_p3 = np.nanmean(rs_p3)
Coefs = np.mean(Matrix , axis = 0)
for dq in range(0, len( approx_model_ttr_p3 .keys)):
approx_model_ttr_p3 .A[ approx_model_ttr_p3 .keys[dq]] = Coefs[dq]
